Real Disjunctions and Ibn Sina's Critique
Rules for real disjunctions and critique of earlier logicians.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the validity of mixed syllogisms involving real disjunctions (haqiqiyya) and reconstruct Ibn Sina's arguments about non-productivity.

Core Idea
A real disjunction (haqiqiyya) is more specific than exclusive or exhaustive; affirmative real disjunctions produce valid conclusions, but negative ones do not.

The author now discusses the real disjunction (haqiqiyya), which combines the properties of exclusive and exhaustive disjunctions. He then presents and defends the views of Ibn Sina (the Sheikh) on several mixed syllogisms that some earlier scholars contested. This lesson will help you understand these subtle rules and the logic behind Ibn Sina's claims.

Guided Path
1
Understand the real disjunction (haqiqiyya)
A real disjunction is one where exactly one of the two parts is true. It is more specific than both exclusive (mani'at al-jam) and exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw) because it combines both properties. The author says it is 'a special case' (أخص) of each.
Remember: exclusive disjunction prevents both being true; exhaustive prevents both being false; real does both.
2
Rule for affirmative real disjunction
An affirmative real disjunction produces the same conclusions as both exclusive and exhaustive disjunctions when used as a major premise in mixed syllogisms. This is because the specific includes the general.
The author says: 'it produces the production of both exclusive and exhaustive'.
3
Rule for negative real disjunction
A negative real disjunction (e.g., 'It is not the case that exactly one of A and B is true') yields no conclusion at all in any of the mentioned mixed syllogisms.
The author explicitly states: 'If it is negative, it produces nothing at all'.
4
Analyze Ibn Sina's claim about non-productivity
Ibn Sina argued that a syllogism with a universal conditional minor and a particular real disjunction major, with middle term as consequent, does not produce a conclusion. The author agrees, explaining that a conclusion must be derived from the terms of the syllogism itself, not from external relations. Even if a conclusion seems to follow via a necessary concomitant, it is not valid as a syllogistic conclusion.
This highlights the importance of the middle term connecting the premises directly.
5
Examine other productive and non-productive cases
Ibn Sina also ruled that a syllogism with a particular negative conditional and a universal affirmative exhaustive disjunction (middle term as antecedent) produces a particular negative conditional. This is valid. On the other hand, a universal affirmative conditional with a universal negative exhaustive disjunction (middle term as consequent) does not produce because the two sides can be both concomitant and contradictory in different situations.
The author affirms these rulings, emphasizing that conversion by contraposition applies to particular affirmatives in many cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ت] وأما القسم الثالث وهو أن تكون المتصلة كبرى والأوسط تاليها فحكمه كحكم القسم الأول إلا في المتصلة الجزئية الموجبة اللازمة من القياس الذي مر ذكره. والمركبة من عين أحد الطرفين ونقيض الآخر فإنها فيه كما في القسم الثاني. [ث] وأما القسم الرابع وهو أن تكون المتصلة كبرى والأوسط مقدمها فحكمه مثل حكم القسم الثاني إلا في المتصلة الجزئية الموجبة اللازمة من القياس الذي مر ذكره المركبة من عين أحد الطرفين ونقيض الآخر كما في القسم الأول: فاعتبر بما عرفت في القسمين الأولين: هذا إذا كانت المنفصلة مانعة الجمع أو مانعة الخلو. فأما إذا كانت حقيقية فإن كانت موجبة فإنه ينتج إنتاج المانعة من الجمع والمانعة من الخلو لكونها أخص من كل واحد منهما حينئذ. والخاص ينتج ما ينتجه العام بالقياس المؤلف من المتصلتين؛ والأوسط فيه عين العام. وإن كانت سالبة فإنه لا ينتج أصلا في شيء من الأقيسة المذكورة يعرف بالتأمل إن شاء الله تعالى. ثم إنه من جملة ما ذهب إليه قوم من المتقدمين على خلاف ما ذهب إليه الجمهور منهم نحو الشيخ وغيره، فإنه قال: «إن الصغرى إذا كانت متصلة كلية، والكبرى منفصلة حقيقية جزئية، والحد الأوسط تالي المتصلة؛ وكلاهما موجبا فإنه لا ينتج». والحق هذا فإنه لا ينتج باعتبار حدوده، والواجب أن تكون النتيجة من حدود القياس وإن كانت ينتج باعتبار الغير لما أن إنتاج حصول الشيء مع اللازم في الجملة مستلزم لامتناع حصوله مع الملزوم. ولا يقال إنه لا يكون مراعيا لهذا في كثير من الصور فإنه لا يجب أن يكون مراعيا إياه في البراهين. وقال أيضا إذا كان القياس مؤلفا من متصلة سالبة جزئية ومنفصلة موجبة كلية مانعا من الخلو؛ والأوسط مقدم المتصلة ينتج سالبة جزئية متصلة من نقيض الأصغر وعين الأكبر وهذا هو الحق أيضا. ولا يظن أن الموجبة الجزئية لا تنعكس عكس النقيض أصلا في اعتقاده بل تنعكس في كثير من الصور كما مر من قبل في موضعه. ولو كان مكان مانعة الخلو مانعة الجمع فإنه ينتج أيضا. وقال أيضا إذا كان القياس مؤلفا من متصلة موجبة كلية وسالبة منفصلة كلية هي مانعة الخلو؛ والأوسط فيه تالي المتصلة، إنه لا ينتج لصدقه مع التلازم بين الطرفين تارة ومع التعاند بينهما أخرى؛ وهذا كما قاله أيضا. ومن تأمل فيما قاله فقد ظهر عليه أنه من جملة ما يمكن تصحيحه؛ ولا طعن فيه.
[T] As for the third division, which is that the conditional is the major and the middle term is its consequent, its ruling is like the first division except in the particular affirmative conditional necessitated from the syllogism that was mentioned. And the compound from the very one of the two terms and the contradictory of the other is in it like the second division. [Th] As for the fourth division, which is that the conditional is the major and the middle term is its antecedent, its ruling is like the second division except in the particular affirmative conditional necessitated from the syllogism that was mentioned, the compound from the very one of the two terms and the contradictory of the other as in the first division. So reflect on what you learned in the first two divisions. This is when the disjunction is exclusive or exhaustive. As for when it is real (haqiqiyya): if it is affirmative, it produces the production of both exclusive and exhaustive because it is more specific than each of them at that time. And the specific produces what the general produces by the syllogism composed of two conditionals; and the middle term in it is the very general. If it is negative, it produces nothing at all in any of the mentioned syllogisms, as will be known by reflection, God willing. Then, among the things held by some earlier scholars contrary to the majority, such as al-Shaykh and others, he said: 'If the minor is a universal conditional, and the major is a particular real disjunction, and the middle term is the consequent of the conditional, and both are affirmative, then it does not produce.' And this is true, for it does not produce with regard to its terms, and the conclusion must be from the terms of the syllogism, even if it produces with regard to something else, because the production of the occurrence of a thing with its necessary concomitant in general entails the impossibility of its occurrence with the condition. And it should not be said that he does not observe this in many cases, for he does not have to observe it in demonstrations. He also said: If the syllogism is composed of a particular negative conditional and a universal affirmative exhaustive disjunction, and the middle term is the antecedent of the conditional, it produces a particular negative conditional from the contradictory of the minor and the very major, and this is also true. And one should not think that the particular affirmative does not convert by contraposition at all in his belief; rather it converts in many cases, as was previously mentioned in its place. And if instead of exhaustive there were exclusive, it also produces. He also said: If the syllogism is composed of a universal affirmative conditional and a universal negative exhaustive disjunction, and the middle term is the consequent of the conditional, it does not produce because it can be true with the two sides being concomitant at one time and contradictory at another. This is as he said. And whoever reflects on what he said will see that it is among what can be corrected; and there is no criticism of it.
Source pages p. 216

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion of mixed syllogisms with conditional and disjunctive premises. The author introduces the third division (middle term = consequent of conditional) and fourth division (middle term = antecedent of conditional), stating that they follow the rules of earlier divisions except for special cases involving particular affirmative conditionals compounded from a term and its contradictory. He then examines real disjunctions (haqiqiyya), which combine both exclusive and exhaustive properties, noting that an affirmative real disjunction produces the same conclusions as both exclusive and exhaustive because it is more specific. A negative real disjunction yields no conclusion. The author then critiques the views of earlier logicians, particularly the Sheikh (Ibn Sina) and his followers, who claimed that no conclusion follows when the minor is a universal conditional and the major a particular real disjunction with the middle term as consequent. The author agrees, arguing that a conclusion must follow from the terms of the syllogism; even if a result holds via an external relation, it is not valid. He defends the Sheikh's other claims, such as the production of a conclusion from a particular negative conditional and a universal positive exhaustive disjunction when the middle term is antecedent, and the non-production when a universal affirmative conditional and universal negative exhaustive disjunction are combined. He emphasizes that conversion by contraposition applies to particular affirmatives in many cases, and that exclusive disjunction behaves similarly.

The Syllogism

The Sixteen Divisions of Mixed Syllogisms
Enumerates sixteen divisions of mixed syllogisms with examples from each figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any mixed syllogism with conditional and disjunctive premises into one of the sixteen divisions and construct a valid example in any figure.

Core Idea
Sixteen mixed syllogisms arise from types of disjunctive, conditional, and position of the shared term.

This lesson unpacks the second division of mixed syllogisms, where the shared term is an incomplete part. The author systematically enumerates sixteen cases based on: (1) whether the disjunctive is exclusive or exhaustive, affirmative or negative; (2) the four types of conditional premises; (3) whether the shared term is the antecedent or consequent of the conditional. You will learn to identify these patterns and apply them in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two premises
One premise is a conditional (muttasila) of the form 'If P then Q' or 'Whenever P then Q'. The other is a disjunctive (munfasila) of the form 'Either... or...', which may be exclusive (mani'at al-jam') or exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw), and each can be affirmative or negative.
2
Determine the type of disjunctive
Check if the disjunctive is exclusive (cannot both be true) or exhaustive (cannot both be false). Also note its quality: affirmative or negative. Example: 'Every C is either H or Z' (exclusive, affirmative).
3
Determine the type of conditional
The conditional can be universal affirmative (e.g., 'Whenever A is B, then every C is D'), universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative. Also note if it is the minor or major premise.
4
Find the shared term
The shared term is a term or proposition that appears in both the conditional and the disjunctive. It is either the antecedent (muqaddam) or the consequent (tali) of the conditional.
5
Formulate the conclusion
The conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent is the non-shared term (the term not shared between the premises) and whose consequent is the result of composing the shared term with the other premise. The conclusion follows the same quality (affirmative/negative) as the disjunctive.
The author states: 'the conclusion in each of the sixteen divisions is the conditional previously mentioned'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني من الأقسام الثلاثة هو الذي يكون الاشتراك في جزء غير تام من كل واحد منهما؛ وإنه ينتج متصلة مقدمها طرف الغير المشارك، وتاليها نتيجة التأليف. ثم المنفصلة إما مانعة الجمع أو مانعة الخلو موجبة كانت كل واحدة منهما أو سالبة. والمتصلة على التقدير الأربعة إما الصغرى أو الكبرى. والطرف المشارك منها للطرف المشارك من المنفصلة إما مقدمها أو تاليها؛ فهذه ستة عشر قسما تنعقد الأشكال الأربعة في كل قسم منها. والضروب فيها هي الضروب في كل شكل. والنتيجة في كل قسم من الأقسام الستة عشر هي المتصلة التي مر ذكرها كما في قولنا: كلما كان أب فكل ج د، ودائما كل ج إما هـ وإما ز ينتج كلما كان أب فكل ج إما هـ وإما ز لانتظام القياس من الطرف المشارك من المتصلة والمنفصلة حينئذ. وكذلك في قولنا: كلما كان أب فكل ج د، وليس البتة شيء من ج إما هـ وإما ز ينتج كلما كان أب فليس البتة شيء من ج إما هـ وإما ز. وأما من الشكل الثاني فذلك مثل قولنا: كلما كان أب فكل ج د، وليس البتة شيء من ج إما هـ أو ز ينتج كلما كان أب فليس البتة شيء من ج إما هـ وإما ز. وعلى هذا في الضروب الباقية من كل شكل هذا إذا كانت الصغرى متصلة. وأما إذا كانت منفصلة فذلك مثل قولنا: دائما إما أن يكون كل أب أو كل ج د، وكلما كان د هـ فـ أو ز ينتج كلما لم يكن كل أب فكلما كان ج د فـ أو ز، فهذا من الشكل الأول. وكذلك إذا قلنا: دائما إما أن يكون كل أب أو كل ج د، وليس البتة إذا كان د هـ فـ أو ز ينتج كلما لم يكن أب فليس البتة إذا كان ج د فـ هـ أو ز. ومن الثاني قولنا: دائما إما أن يكون كل أب أو كل ج د، وليس البتة إذا كان هـ د فـ و ز ينتج النتيجة المذكورة. وعلى هذا في الضروب الباقية من كل شكل والبرهان في الكل هو الذي مر ذكره من قبل. ومن زعم بأنه كما ينتج المتصلة التي بين وصفها فكذلك ينتج المنفصلة من جنس المنفصلة المستعملة موافقة إياها في الكيف مركبة من الطرف الغير المشارك منها، ومن نتيجة التأليف بين الطرف المشارك والمتصلة. فلا نزاع
The second of the three divisions is that in which the common element is an incomplete part from each of them; and it yields a conditional whose antecedent is the non‑shared term and whose consequent is the result of the composition. Then the disjunctive is either exclusive disjunction or exhaustive disjunction, each being either affirmative or negative. The conditional on the four assumptions is either minor or major. The shared term from it with the shared term from the disjunctive is either its antecedent or its consequent; so these are sixteen divisions, in each of which the four figures are constituted. The moods therein are the moods in each figure. The conclusion in each of the sixteen divisions is the conditional previously mentioned, as in our saying: Whenever A is B, then every C is D, and always every C is either H or Z, yields 'Whenever A is B, then every C is either H or Z' because the syllogism is then ordered from the shared term of the conditional and the disjunctive. And similarly in our saying: Whenever A is B, then every C is D, and never is anything from C either H or Z, yields 'Whenever A is B, then never is anything from C either H or Z'. As for the second figure, that is like our saying: Whenever A is B, then every C is D, and never is anything from C either H or Z, yields 'Whenever A is B, then never is anything from C either H or Z'. And so on for the remaining moods of each figure, this when the minor is conditional. As for when it is disjunctive, that is like our saying: Always either every A is B or every C is D, and whenever D is H then or Z, yields 'Whenever not every A is B, then whenever C is D then or Z'; this is from the first figure. And similarly if we say: Always either every A is B or every C is D, and never when D is H then or Z, yields 'Whenever not A is B, then never when C is D then H or Z'. And from the second, our saying: Always either every A is B or every C is D, and never when H is D then W and Z, yields the mentioned conclusion. And so on for the remaining moods of each figure, and the proof for all is what was previously mentioned. And whoever claims that just as it yields the conditional described, so too it yields a disjunctive of the same kind as the disjunctive used, agreeing with it in quality, composed of the non‑shared term and the result of the composition between the shared term and the conditional—there is no dispute.
Source pages p. 217

Commentary
This passage explains the second of three divisions of mixed syllogisms (qiyas murakkab) combining conditional (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila) premises. The author first defines the characteristic of this division: the common element (al-ishtirak) is an incomplete part from each premise. The conclusion is a conditional whose antecedent is the non‑shared term and whose consequent is the result of combining the shared terms. The disjunctive premise can be either exclusive (mani‘at al-jam‘) or exhaustive (mani‘at al-khuluw), affirmative or negative. The conditional premise has four possibilities (affirmative/negative, universal/particular), and it may be the minor or major premise. Moreover, the shared term between the conditional and the disjunctive may be the antecedent or consequent of the conditional. This yields sixteen possible divisions (2 kinds of disjunctive × 4 conditionals × 2 positions of shared term). In each division, the four figures of the categorical syllogism are applicable, and the moods are the standard moods for each figure. The author then provides concrete examples using the variables أ, ب, ج, د, هـ, ز (which stand for arbitrary terms/propositions). The first example: 'Whenever A is B, then every C is D; and always every C is either H or Z' yields the conclusion 'Whenever A is B, then every C is either H or Z'. This is an instance where the shared term is 'C' which appears in both premises. The second example uses a negative disjunctive: 'never is anything from C either H or Z' yields a negative conclusion. Then an example from the second figure is given. The author then explains the case when the minor premise is disjunctive (i.e., the disjunctive is the minor and the conditional is the major). For example: 'Always either every A is B or every C is D; and whenever D is H then or Z' yields 'Whenever not every A is B, then whenever C is D then or Z' (first figure). The author continues with another example from the second figure. He asserts that the proof for all these moods is the same as previously established. Finally, he mentions that there is no dispute among scholars about these results, especially regarding the claim that the conclusion could also be a disjunctive premise of the same kind as the original disjunctive, composed of the non‑shared term and the result of the composition — this is accepted.

A) A conditional whose antecedent is the shared term and consequent is the disjunction of the non-shared terms.
B) A conditional whose antecedent is the non-shared term and consequent is the shared term.
C) A conditional whose antecedent is the non-shared term and consequent is the result of the composition (e.g., 'Whenever A is B, then every C is either H or Z').
D) A disjunctive whose parts are the non-shared terms.

Key Takeaway
In mixed syllogisms with conditional and disjunctive premises, the conclusion is a conditional with the non-shared term as antecedent and the composition result as consequent, across sixteen possible divisions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Let the disjunctive be 'Every month is either Ramadan or Shawwal.' Derive the conclusion and identify which of the sixteen divisions it belongs to.

The Syllogism

The Sixteen Divisions of Compound Conditional-Disjunctive Syllogisms
Author enumerates 16 divisions of compound syllogisms from a conditional and a disjunctive premise, with examples from first and second figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the sixteen divisions of compound syllogisms formed from a conditional and a disjunctive premise, and reconstruct the conclusion pattern for each combination.

Core Idea
Sixteen divisions arise from combining four figures with four premise-order variations.

Compound syllogisms can combine a conditional (if-then) premise with a disjunctive (either-or) premise. The author systematically enumerates all possible combinations across the four syllogistic figures, showing how the conclusion follows. Understanding this enumeration helps you see how Islamic logicians structured complex arguments combining different proposition types, as encountered in usul al-fiqh when conditional rulings are combined with exclusive alternatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise types
Note that one premise is a conditional (muttasila: 'If A then B') and the other is a disjunctive (munfasila: 'Either C or D'). The order of premises (major/minor) can vary.
In the examples, the minor premise is conditional first, then disjunctive.
2
Determine the figure (shakl)
The figure is determined by the position of the shared term linking the two premises. The author gives examples from the first and second figures.
First figure: middle term is subject in major and predicate in minor. Second figure: middle term is predicate in both.
3
Extract the shared term
Find the term that appears in both premises; it serves as the middle term that guarantees the conclusion. In the first example, 'C' is shared.
Use the variable mapping (أب، ج، د، هـ، ز) to track terms.
4
Derive the conclusion (natija)
The conclusion combines the conditional 'if...' part from the conditional premise with the disjunctive 'either...or' part from the disjunctive premise, linked via the shared term.
The conclusion is always a conditional whose consequent is a disjunction or negative of disjunction.
5
Recognize the sixteen divisions
Four figures × four premise-order combinations (conditional major/minor, disjunctive major/minor, and affirmative/negative variations) yield sixteen divisions.
Each division has its own valid moods, but the author only illustrates some.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه ستة عشر قسما تنعقد الأشكال الأربعة في كل قسم منها. والضروب فيها هي الضروب في كل شكل. والنتيجة في كل قسم من الأقسام الستة عشر هي المتّصلة التي مرَّ ذكرها كما في قولنا كلَّما كان «أب» فكل «ج د»، ودائما كل «ج» إما «هـ» وإما «ز» ينتج كلَّما كان «أب» فكل «ج» إما «ه» وإما «ز» لانتظام القياس من الطرف المشارك من المتّصلة والمنفصلة حينئذ. وكذلك في قولنا كلَّما كان (أب) فكل (ج) د ، وليس البتة شيء من «ج» إما «هـ» وإما «ز» ينتج كلَّما كان «أب» فليس البتة شيء من «ج» إما «هـ» وإما «ز». وأما من الشكل الثاني فذلك مثل قولنا كلما كان (أ ب) فكل ( د)، وليس البتة شيء من «ه» أواز، دا ينتج كلما كان «أب» فليس البتة شيء من (ج أ» إما «ه» وإما «ز». وعلى هذا في الضروب الباقية من كل شكل هذا إذا كانت الصغرى متّصلة. وأما إذا كانت منفصلة فذلك مثل قولنا دائما إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو كل (ج) د ، وكلَّما كان دهـا فـ او ز ينتج كلما لم يكن كل «أب» فكلما كان جد» فـ او ز»، فهذا من الشكل الأول. وكذلك إذا قلنا دائما إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو كل ج د»، وليس البتة إذا كان ده فـ «اوز» ينتج كلما لم يكن «أب» فليس البتة إذا كان (ج) (ب) فـ ( و ) . ومن الثاني قولنا دائما إما أن يكون كل «أب» أو كل ج د ، وليس البتة إذا كان هـد فـ و ز ينتج النتيجة المذكورة. وعلى هذا في الضروب الباقية من كل شكل والبرهان في الكل هو الذي مرَّ ذكره من قبل.
Thus these are sixteen divisions; the four figures are formed in each division. The moods in them are the moods in each figure. The conclusion in each of the sixteen divisions is the conditional that was mentioned earlier, as in our statement: 'Whenever A is B, then every C is D', and 'Always every C is either H or Z' yields 'Whenever A is B, then every C is either H or Z', because the syllogism is constructed from the shared term of the conditional and the disjunctive at that time. And likewise in our statement: 'Whenever A is B, then every C is D', and 'Absolutely nothing of C is either H or Z' yields 'Whenever A is B, then absolutely nothing of C is either H or Z'. As for the second figure, that is like our statement: 'Whenever A is B, then every D', and 'Absolutely nothing of C is either H or Z' yields 'Whenever A is B, then absolutely nothing of [C?] is either H or Z'. And similar [arguments apply] for the remaining moods of each figure, when the minor premise is a conditional. But when it is a disjunctive, that is like our statement: 'Always either every A is B or every C is D', and 'Whenever D is H, then Z' yields 'Whenever not every A is B, then whenever C is D, then Z'; this is from the first figure. And likewise if we say: 'Always either every A is B or every C is D', and 'It is absolutely not the case that if D is H, then Z' yields 'Whenever not every A is B, then it is absolutely not the case that if C is B, then W'. And from the second: our statement: 'Always either every A is B or every C is D', and 'It is absolutely not the case that if H is D, then W and Z' yields the mentioned conclusion. And similar [arguments apply] for the remaining moods of each figure. And the proof for all is what was mentioned before.

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and explanation of the sixteen divisions of compound syllogisms formed from a conditional (muttasila) and a disjunctive (munfasila) premise. The author begins by stating that there are sixteen divisions, and within each division the four syllogistic figures apply, each with its own valid moods. The conclusion of each division is a conditional, as previously defined. The author then provides examples from the first and second figures, first when the minor premise is a conditional, and then when it is a disjunctive. The examples use placeholder variables (أ ب ج د هـ و ز) to illustrate the logical structure. For instance, when the minor is 'Whenever A is B, then every C is D' and the major is 'Always every C is either H or Z', the conclusion is 'Whenever A is B, then every C is either H or Z'. This works because the shared term (C) links the two premises. Similarly, when the major is negative, the conclusion becomes negative. The author then shows analogous examples for the second figure. After illustrating cases where the minor is conditional, the author turns to cases where the minor is disjunctive, providing examples from the first and second figures as well. The passage concludes by noting that the proof for all these cases is the same as what was mentioned earlier in the text. This enumeration serves to systematically cover all possible combinations of conditional and disjunctive premises in compound syllogisms.

Objection on Producing a Disjunctive Conclusion from Compound Syllogisms
Author addresses an objection about whether the conclusion can be a disjunctive when combining a conditional and a disjunctive premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a disjunctive conclusion follows from a compound syllogism involving a conditional and a disjunctive premise.

Core Idea
Not every disjunctive premise yields a disjunctive conclusion – it must be universal and of a specific type.

This passage refines a claim about compound syllogisms: some argue that combining a conditional and a disjunctive always yields a disjunctive conclusion. The author agrees but imposes two key conditions: the disjunctive must be universal and must be either mutually exclusive (mani'at al-jam') or exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw). These conditions ensure the inference is valid, and we will break them down step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refined
Find in the Arabic where 'someone claims' that a disjunctive premise yields a disjunctive conclusion of the same kind used, agreeing in quality, composed of the non-shared term and the result of composing the shared term with the conditional.
The claim is about compound syllogisms where one premise is conditional (muttasil) and the other is disjunctive (munfasil).
2
Extract the author's conditions
The author states: 'it is required that the result of the composition with the conditional be productive for the shared term of the disjunctive if the disjunctive is exclusive of both (mani'at al-jam') or exclusive of emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw)'. Also 'the disjunctive must be universal (kuliyya)'.
Two conditions: (1) the disjunctive type must be mani'at al-jam' or mani'at al-khuluw, (2) it must be universal.
3
Understand the disjunctive types
Mani'at al-jam' means both disjuncts cannot be true together; mani'at al-khuluw means both cannot be false together. A 'real disjunctive' (haqiqiyya) is both. The author explains that if the disjunctive is real and affirmative, it yields an affirmative disjunctive conclusion of the same type.
If the disjunctive is negative, no conclusion follows; otherwise a non-real negative would be productive, which is impossible.
4
Apply the conditions to test validity
Take a concrete example from Islamic law: Conditional: 'If the contract is valid, then it is binding.' Disjunctive: 'Either the contract is valid or the contract is void' (a real disjunctive, universal). Does a disjunctive conclusion follow? Check the conditions: universal? Yes. Type? It is haqiqiyya, which includes both mani'at al-jam' and mani'at al-khuluw. Affirmative? Yes. Therefore, the conclusion should be an affirmative disjunctive: 'Either the contract is binding or the contract is void.' That seems valid. Now test a negative or non-universal disjunctive and see it fails.
The author's condition prevents invalid inferences where the disjunctive is not of the required types.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن زعم بأنه كما ينتج المتّصلة التي بين وصفها فكذلك ينتج المنفصلة من جنس المنفصلة المستعملة موافقة إياها في الكيف مركبة من الطرف الغير المشارك منها، ومن نتيجة التأليف بين الطرف المشارك والمتصلة. فلا نزاع معه لكن يشترط أن تكون نتيجة التأليف مع المتّصلة منتجة للطرف المشارك المنفصلة إذا كانت المنفصلة مانعة الجمع أو مانعة الخلو. ثم المنفصلة لا بد وأن تكون كلية في هذه الاقسام؛ وإنها إذا كانت حقيقية، فإن كانت موجبة فإنه ينتج موجبة مانعة الجمع أو مانعة الخلو . ولا ينتج إذا كانت سالبة وإلا لكانت السالبة اللا حقيقية منتجة؛ وذلك ممتنع يعرف بالتأمل إن شاء الله تعالى.
And whoever claims that just as the conditional yields [a conclusion] of the type described, so too the disjunctive yields a disjunctive of the same kind used, agreeing with it in quality, composed of the non-shared term and the result of the composition between the shared term and the conditional — there is no dispute with him, but it is required that the result of the composition with the conditional be productive for the shared term of the disjunctive if the disjunctive is exclusive of both (mani'at al-jam') or exclusive of emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw). Further, the disjunctive must be universal in these divisions; and if it is real (haqiqiyya), then if it is affirmative, it yields an affirmative [conclusion] that is exclusive of both or exclusive of emptiness. But it does not yield [a conclusion] if it is negative; otherwise the non-real negative would be productive, and that is impossible, as can be known by reflection, God willing.
Source pages p. 218

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement addressing an objection. Someone claims that from a combination of a conditional and a disjunctive premise, the conclusion can be a disjunctive of the same type as the major premise, composed of the non-shared term and the result of composing the shared term with the conditional. The author agrees but imposes a condition: the disjunctive premise must be either 'mani'at al-jam'' (exclusive of both, i.e., they cannot both be true) or 'mani'at al-khuluw' (exclusive of emptiness, i.e., they cannot both be false). Additionally, the disjunctive premise must be universal (kuliyya). If the disjunctive is 'haqiqiyya' (real, i.e., both actual), then if it is affirmative, the conclusion will be an affirmative disjunction of the same type. However, if the disjunctive is negative, no conclusion is produced; otherwise, a non-real negative would be productive, which is impossible. The author asserts this can be known through reflection. This passage fine-tunes the conditions for valid compound syllogisms involving disjunctives.

Key Terms
مانعة الجمع (mani'at al-jam'): A disjunction where both disjuncts cannot be true simultaneously (mutually exclusive).
مانعة الخلو (mani'at al-khuluw): A disjunction where both disjuncts cannot be false simultaneously (exhaustive, at least one true).
حقيقية (haqiqiyya): A real disjunction that is both exclusive and exhaustive (the standard 'either-or' in logic).
كلية (kuliyya): Universal, quantified over all instances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) It must be non-real (la haqiqiyya) and affirmative.
D) It must be universal and real.

Key Takeaway
A disjunctive conclusion from a compound syllogism combining a conditional and a disjunctive premise requires the disjunctive to be universal and of the exclusive (mani'at al-jam') or exhaustive (mani'at al-khuluw) type.

Third Type: Middle Term Perfect in One, Imperfect in the Other
Author classifies the third type of compound syllogism with twenty subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third type of compound syllogism where the middle term is perfect in one premise and imperfect in the other, and classify its twenty subdivisions.

Core Idea
The third type of compound syllogism reduces to a categorical-disjunctive structure with twenty subdivisions.

The previous lessons covered compound syllogisms where the middle term is complete in both premises or incomplete in both. The third type introduces asymmetry: the middle term is perfect (full part) in one premise but imperfect (partial part) in the other. This occurs when one premise's extreme is a conditional, sharing an imperfect part with the other premise. This lesson unpacks the resulting twenty subdivisions and shows how they all reduce to a syllogism of categorical and disjunctive premises, with the conditional acting as the categorical.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Asymmetry Condition
The middle term is perfect in one premise and imperfect in the other. This asymmetry requires that one premise's extreme be a conditional proposition. Explain in your own words why the conditional forces this asymmetry.
Contrast with the first two types where the middle term is either perfect in both or imperfect in both.
2
Classify the Perfect Part
The perfect part (the middle term) is either taken from a conditional (muttasila) or a disjunctive (munfasila). This yields two main divisions. Write down the two base categories.
3
Trace the Combinatorics
The author subdivides further: for the case where the perfect part is from a disjunctive, the disjunctive may be affirmative in all parts, negative exclusive of both (mani'at al-jam'), or negative exclusive of emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw). Then the conditional can be minor or major, and the conditional itself can be muttasila or munfasila. Attempt to reconstruct the count of twenty subdivisions.
The full combinatorics: 2 (perfect part type) × [3 (disjunctive polarities) × 2 (conditional type) × 2 (position)]? But the author states twenty total.
4
Analyze the Examples
The author gives two examples when the conditional is minor: one yields a conclusion of type 'mani'at al-khuluw', the other 'mani'at al-jam'. Using the logical notation (أ, ب, ج, د, ح, ط, هـ), identify the perfect and imperfect parts and follow the derivation.
Example 1: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D' and 'Always either whenever C is D, then H is T or H is D as exclusive of emptiness' yields 'Whenever A is B, then C is T as exclusive of emptiness'.
5
Generalize to the Major Case
The third example (conditional as major) is partially given. Based on the pattern, predict what the conclusion should be when the conditional is major.

As for the third type — that the middle term in it is perfect in one of the two premises and imperfect in the other. This is conceivable only when one of the two terminals of one premise is a conditional; that conditional shares with the other premise an imperfect part in each of them. The perfect part is either from the conditional or from the disjunctive, so it falls into two divisions: [1] The first division is either that the disjunctive in it is affirmative in all its parts, or negative exclusive of both, or negative exclusive of emptiness. And in all cases, the conditional is either a conditional or a disjunctive; in all cases, the conditional may be minor or major. Thus these are twenty divisions, all of which reduce to the syllogism composed of categorical and disjunctive; the conditional takes the place of the categorical. The conditions are those that were mentioned in that [section]. And the conclusions are likewise, as in our statement with the conditional as minor: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D', and 'Always either whenever C is D, then H is T' or 'H is D' as exclusive of emptiness, yields 'Whenever A is B, then C is T' as exclusive of emptiness, because of what has been mentioned. And like our statement: 'Whenever A is B, then C is D', and 'Absolutely not either whenever C is D, then H is T, or H is Z' as exclusive of both, yields 'Absolutely not either H is Z or whenever A is B, then C is T' as exclusive of both, because the permissibility of combination with the necessary entails the permissibility of combination with the necessary. And if the conditional is major, that is like our statement: 'Always either H is Z or whenever A is B, then C is D' as exclusive of emptiness, and 'Whenever C is D, then H is T'

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the third type of compound syllogism, where the middle term is perfect in one premise and imperfect in the other. The author explains that this occurs only when one of the extremes of a premise is a conditional, which shares an imperfect part with the other premise. The perfect part (the middle term) can be from either a conditional or a disjunctive, leading to two main divisions. Under the first division, the disjunctive can be affirmative in all parts, or negative in two varieties (mani'at al-jam' or mani'at al-khuluw). A third example is partially given for when the conditional is major. This passage systematically breaks down a complex type of compound syllogism.

Key Terms
وسط (wasat): Middle term of the syllogism, here described as 'perfect' (tamm) in one premise and 'imperfect' (ghayr tamm) in the other, referring to its completeness as a term.
شرطية (shartiyya): A conditional proposition (either muttasila or munfasila).
جزء تام (juz' tamm): Perfect part, i.e., the middle term that is fully present in one premise.
جزء غير تام (juz' ghayr tamm): Imperfect part, i.e., the part that is shared but not the complete term.

The Twenty Cases of Mixed Syllogisms
Author enumerates twenty cases of mixed syllogisms with disjunctive as complete part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the twenty cases of mixed syllogisms where the complete part is disjunctive and explain how they reduce to a categorical-disjunctive form.

Core Idea
Mixed syllogisms with an incomplete premise require a conditional; twenty cases arise from three disjunctive types, two conditional types, and two positions.

This passage introduces a systematic classification of mixed syllogisms where one premise is complete (disjunctive) and the other incomplete. The author breaks down the possibilities by type of disjunctive (affirmative, negative exclusive of conjunction, negative exclusive of void), type of conditional (connected or disjunctive), and whether the conditional is minor or major, yielding exactly twenty cases. Understanding this structure helps you recognise the building blocks of complex logical arguments used in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the complete and incomplete premises
In a mixed syllogism, the middle term is complete in one premise and incomplete in the other. Here the complete premise is always disjunctive (munfasila). Locate which premise has the full disjunctive: it can be affirmative, mani'at al-jam', or mani'at al-khuluw.
2
Determine the type of conditional
The incomplete premise contains a conditional (shartiyya). Decide whether it is connected (muttasila) or disjunctive (munfasila). This gives two possibilities for each disjunctive type.
3
Check the position of the connected premise
If the incomplete premise is connected, it can be either the minor premise or the major premise. Multiply the previous possibilities by 2.
4
Calculate the total cases
Count: 3 disjunctive types × 2 conditional types × 2 positions = 12 cases? But the author says 20. The extra cases come from the fact that the conditional itself can be disjunctive, adding more subcases. The text explicitly says: the disjunctive types (3) × conditional types (2) × position (2) × (connected or disjunctive) = 3×2×2×? Actually the text says 'فهذه عشرون قسما' so accept the author's count.
The exact breakdown is not fully elaborated, but the key is that all reduce to categorical-disjunctive syllogisms.
5
Reduce to the basic form
Note that the connected conditional takes the place of the categorical premise. See how the examples later convert the conditional into a categorical-like proposition.

As for the third division, which is that the middle term is complete in one premise and incomplete in the other; this can only be conceived if one of the two parts of one premise is conditional; that conditional shares with the other premise an incomplete part from each of them; and the complete part is either from the conditional or from the disjunctive, so it is of two divisions: [1] The first division: either the disjunctive is affirmative in all its parts, or negative exclusive of conjunction, or negative exclusive of void. Commentary
This passage discusses the third division of syllogisms where the middle term is complete in one premise but not in the other. All these reduce to syllogisms composed of categorical and disjunctive premises, with the connected taking the place of the categorical. The reasoning relies on the principle that permissibility of conjunction with the required term entails permissibility with the necessary term. The passage concludes by stating that the same logic applies to the remaining modes.

Validating Mixed Syllogisms with Connected Premises
Author illustrates two examples of mixed syllogisms with connected premises as minor and major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and validate mixed syllogisms where the connected conditional is either minor or major, using the permissibility of conjunction principle.

Core Idea
The permissibility of conjunction principle enables valid inference in mixed syllogisms with disjunctive premises.

The author provides two kinds of examples: one with the connected conditional as minor, and one with it as major. Each uses a disjunctive premise of a specific type (exclusive of void or exclusive of conjunction). By working through these examples, you will learn how to derive the conclusion and why it follows from the stated conditions. This skill is essential for constructing compound syllogisms in Islamic legal and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disjunctive premise
In each example, first determine if the disjunctive is exclusive of void (mani'at al-khuluw) or exclusive of conjunction (mani'at al-jam'). This affects the conclusion.
2
Locate the connected conditional
The connected conditional (muttasila) takes the place of a categorical. Determine if it is minor or major premise. This changes how the premise combines with the disjunctive.
3
Express the syllogism in abstract form
Write the premises using variables (e.g., AB, CD, HZ, etc.). For the first example: connected minor: whenever AB is CD; disjunctive major: always either whenever CD is HZ or (HD) exclusive of void. Conclusion: whenever AB is CT exclusive of void.
4
Apply the permissibility of conjunction principle
The author notes that the conclusion follows because 'permissibility of conjunction with the requisite entails permissibility with the necessary.' Understand how this principle ensures that the conjunction of the conclusion's parts is allowed if the premises allow it.
This is a crucial logical rule for these syllogisms.
5
Repeat for the major connected example
The second set places the connected as major: disjunctive minor: always either HE or whenever AB is CD exclusive of void; connected major: whenever CD is HZ. Conclusion resembles the first example. Check that the same principle applies.

وأما القسم الثالث وهو أن يكون الأوسط فيه تاما في إحدى المقدمتين غير تام في الأخرى؛ وذلك لا يتصور إلا وأن يكون أحد طرفي إحدى المقدمتين شرطية؛ تلك الشرطية متشاركة مع المقدمة الأخرى في جزء غير تام من كل واحدة منهما؛ والجزء التام إما أن يكون من المتصلة أو من المنفصلة فيكون على قسمين: [1] والقسم الأول إما أن تكون المنفصلة فيه موجبة بجميع أجزائها أو سالبة مانعة الجمع أو سالبة مانعة الخلو. وعلى التقادير فالشرطية إما متصلة أو منفصلة على التقادير إما أن تكون المتصلة صغرى أو كبرى؛ فهذه عشرون قسما يرجع كلها إلى القياس المؤلف من الحملية والمنفصلة؛ والمتصلة فيها مكان الحملية. والشرائط هي التي مرَّ ذكرها في ذلك. والنتائج كذلك كما في قولنا؛ والمتصلة صغرى كلَّما كان «أب» فـ «ج د»، ودائمًا إما كلَّما كان «ج د» ف ح ط» وإما ( هـ د» مانعا من الخلو ينتج كلَّما كان «أب» فـ «ج ط» مانعا من الخلو لما مر. وكقولنا كلما كان «أب» فـ اج دا ، وليس البتة إما كلما كان (ج) د فحط وإما «هز» مانعا من الجمع ينتج ليس البتة إما «هز» وإما كلَّما كان (أب) فـ (ج) ط» مانعا من الجمع لاستلزام جواز الجمع مع الملزوم جواز الجمع مع اللازم. وإن كانت المتصلة كبرى فذلك مثل قولنا دائما إما أن يكون ه ز» أو كلَّما كان «أب» فـ (ج) د مانعا من الخلو، وكلَّما كان جد» فـ ح ط» ينتج النتيجة المذكورة في المثال الأول لما مر . وكقولنا ليس البتة إما أن يكون هز» أو كلما كان «أب» فـ (ج) دا ، وكلَّما كان «ج ده فـاح ط» مانعا من الجمع ينتج النتيجة المذكورة في المثال الثاني. وعلى هذا في الغير من الضروب الباقية.
As for the third division, which is that the middle term is complete in one premise and incomplete in the other; this can only be conceived if one of the two parts of one premise is conditional; that conditional shares with the other premise an incomplete part from each of them; and the complete part is either from the conditional or from the disjunctive, so it is of two divisions: [1] The first division: either the disjunctive is affirmative in all its parts, or negative exclusive of conjunction, or negative exclusive of void. In all cases, the conditional is either connected or disjunctive; in all cases, the connected may be minor or major; these are twenty cases, all of which reduce to the syllogism composed of categorical and disjunctive; the connected takes the place of the categorical. The conditions are those mentioned earlier. The conclusions are likewise, as in our statement: the connected as minor: 'Whenever AB is CD, and always either whenever CD is HZ or (HD) is exclusive of void', it yields 'Whenever AB is CT exclusive of void' due to what passed. And like our statement: 'Whenever AB is CD, and not at all either whenever CD is HZ or HE is exclusive of conjunction', it yields 'Not at all either HE or whenever AB is CT exclusive of conjunction' because permissibility of conjunction with the requisite entails permissibility of conjunction with the necessary. And if the connected is major, that is like our statement: 'Always either HE or whenever AB is CD exclusive of void, and whenever CD is HZ', it yields the conclusion mentioned in the first example due to what passed. And like our statement: 'Not at all either HE or whenever AB is CD, and whenever CD is HZ exclusive of conjunction', it yields the conclusion mentioned in the second example. And likewise for the remaining modes.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage discusses the third division of syllogisms where the middle term is complete in one premise but not in the other. The author explains that such a situation arises only when one premise contains a conditional proposition that shares a partial component with the other premise. The complete part can be either connected (muttasila) or disjunctive (munfasila). The author then details the first sub-division where the complete part is disjunctive. The disjunctive can be affirmative, negative exclusive of conjunction (mani'at al-jam'), or negative exclusive of void (mani'at al-khuluw). In each case, the conditional can be connected or disjunctive, and can be minor or major, yielding twenty total cases. All these reduce to syllogisms composed of categorical and disjunctive premises, with the connected taking the place of the categorical. The author provides two examples: one with the connected as minor and another with it as major, demonstrating how the conclusion follows from the stated conditions. The reasoning relies on the principle that permissibility of conjunction with the required term entails permissibility with the necessary term. The passage concludes by stating that the same logic applies to the remaining modes.

Key Terms

شرطية منفصلة
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو

Checkpoint
In the first example of the passage (connected as minor, disjunctive exclusive of void), what is the conclusion?
A) Whenever AB is CT exclusive of void
B) Not at all either HE or whenever AB is CT exclusive of conjunction
C) Always either HE or whenever AB is CT exclusive of void
D) Whenever AB is CT exclusive of conjunction

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of conjunction principle is the key to validating mixed syllogisms with disjunctive premises; it ensures that the conclusion's combination is logically allowed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the second example (connected as minor with exclusive of conjunction) and replace the abstract variables with a concrete Islamic legal scenario: e.g., 'Whenever a contract contains gharar (AB), it is invalid (CD). And not at all either whenever a contract is invalid (CD), it is rescindable (HZ) or it is void ab initio (HE) exclusive of conjunction.' Write the conclusion and verify if it matches the pattern.
?
Construct a new mixed syllogism where the connected is major and the disjunctive is exclusive of void, using terms from kalam: e.g., 'Always either the world is temporally originated (HE) or whenever the world is temporally originated (AB), it is contingent (CD) exclusive of void. And whenever the world is contingent (CD), it has a cause (HZ).' Derive the conclusion.

The Sixteen Cases of Mixed Conditional Syllogisms
Enumeration of sixteen cases via four binary factors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the four binary factors that generate the sixteen cases of mixed conditional syllogisms in the second division.

Core Idea
Sixteen cases arise from four binary choices: quality, type, position, and role.

The author systematically enumerates all possible forms of a mixed conditional syllogism where one premise is a connected conditional (muttasila). Understanding this enumeration is the first step to mastering compound syllogisms and applying them to Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first binary factor
The connected conditional can be affirmative or negative (2 possibilities).
Quality of the muttasila
2
Identify the second binary factor
The other premise (the incomplete part) can be a connected conditional or a disjunctive conditional (2 possibilities).
Type of the other conditional
3
Identify the third binary factor
That other conditional can be the antecedent (muqaddam) or the consequent (tali) of the connected (2 possibilities).
Position relative to the connected
4
Identify the fourth binary factor
The connected conditional can be the minor or major premise (2 possibilities).
Role in the syllogism
5
Multiply the possibilities
2 × 2 × 2 × 2 = 16 cases. All reduce to a standard form.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ب] وأما الثاني من القسمين فالمتصلة فيه إما أن تكون موجبة أو سالبة. وعلى التقديرين فتلك الشرطية إما متّصلة أو منفصلة. وعلى التقادير فهي إما مقدم المتصلة أو تاليها وعلى التقادير فالمتصلة إما الصغرى أو الكبرى فهذه ستة عشر قسما يرجع كلها إلى القياس المؤلف من الحملية والمتصلة) والمنفصلة فيها مكان الحملية والشرائط هي التي مرَّ ذكرها والنتائج فيها هي النتائج فيما يرجع إليه على أن تكون المنفصلة فيها موضوعة في القياس مكان الحملية هناك فيه ونتيجة التأليف من الشرطيتين في النتيجة مكان نتيجة التأليف من الحمليتين فيها كما في قولنا؛ والمتصلة صغرى كلما كان «ج د» فــ هــز فـ ح ط»، ودائمًا إما أن يكون «هـ ز » أو «أب» مانعا من الجمع ينتج قد يكون إذا كان دائما إما (ج) د) أو (ب (أ) مانعا من الجمع فاح ط». وكقولنا في الكبرى والصغرى بحالها ليس البتة إما أن يكون هز» أو «أب» مانعا من الخلو ينتج قد يكون إذا كان ليس البتة إما جد» أو «أب» مانعا من الخلو ف «ح ط»: هذا إذا كانت المتصلة صغرى. فأما إذا كانت كبرى فذلك كقولنا دائما إما أن يكون «أب) أو (ج د مانعا من الخلو، وكلَّما كان جدا فـ «هز» فـاح ط» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان إما «أب» أو «هز» مانعا من الخلو فـ «ح ط». وكقولنا في الصغرى والكبرى بحالها ليس البتة إما «أب» أو «هز» مانعا من الجمع ـ ح . والبيان في الكل هو الذي مرَّ ذكره. وعلى هذا في الضروب فـ ط الباقية من كل قسم؛ فاعتبر بما عرفت، وبالله التوفيق.
[B] As for the second of the two divisions, the connected therein is either affirmative or negative. In both cases, that conditional is either connected or disjunctive. In all cases, it is either the antecedent or the consequent of the connected. In all cases, the connected is either minor or major; these are sixteen cases, all of which reduce to the syllogism composed of categorical and connected, with the disjunctive taking the place of the categorical. The conditions are those mentioned earlier. The conclusions in them are the conclusions in what they reduce to, such that the disjunctive is placed in the syllogism in the place of the categorical there, and the result of composition of the two conditionals in the conclusion takes the place of the result of composition of the two categoricals there, as in our statement: The connected as minor: 'Whenever CD is HZ, then HT, and always either HZ or AB is exclusive of conjunction', it yields 'It may be that if always either CD or A-B is exclusive of conjunction, then HT'. And like our statement with the major and minor as they are: 'Not at all either HZ or AB is exclusive of void', it yields 'It may be that if not at all either CD or AB is exclusive of void, then HT'. This is when the connected is minor. As for when it is major, that is like our statement: 'Always either AB or CD is exclusive of void, and whenever CD is HZ then HT', it yields 'It may be that if either AB or HZ is exclusive of void, then HT'. And like our statement with the minor and major as they are: 'Not at all either AB or HZ is exclusive of conjunction' - HT. The explanation for all is what has been mentioned. And likewise for the remaining modes from each division; so consider what you have come to know, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 219

Commentary
This passage covers the second division of the third main division, where the complete part is from the connected (muttasila) rather than the disjunctive. The author first enumerates the variables: the connected can be affirmative or negative (2 possibilities); the conditional (the incomplete part) can be connected or disjunctive (2); and it can be either the antecedent or the consequent of the connected (2); and the connected can be minor or major (2). This yields 2×2×2×2 = 16 cases. All reduce to a syllogism composed of a categorical and a connected, where the disjunctive takes the place of the categorical in the reduced form. The author then provides examples: one with the connected as minor, and one with it as major. In the first example, the connected is minor, and the major premise is a disjunctive exclusive of conjunction. The conclusion is a conditional statement expressing possibility. In the second example, the connected is major, and the minor premise is disjunctive. The author emphasizes that the reasoning is analogous to what was mentioned earlier. The passage concludes with an admonition to reflect on what has been learned.

Reduction of Mixed Conditionals to Standard Form
Explanation of reduction principle for mixed conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reduce any of the sixteen mixed conditional syllogisms to a syllogism composed of a categorical and a connected conditional.

Core Idea
Every mixed conditional syllogism reduces to a form where the disjunctive takes the place of the categorical.

The author explains a key reduction: all complex mixed conditionals can be transformed into a simpler syllogism. This reduction clarifies the logical structure and allows us to apply known validity conditions from earlier discussions. Understanding this reduction is essential for constructing valid arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise
In the original mixed syllogism, one premise is a disjunctive conditional (either connected or disjunctive). In the reduction, this disjunctive plays the role of the categorical premise.
The disjunctive replaces the categorical in the reduced form.
2
Treat the disjunctive as a categorical
For the purpose of reduction, consider the disjunctive as if it were a categorical proposition (e.g., 'All A is B' equivalents are not used here; rather, the disjunctive structure is mapped to a categorical mood).
The author implies that the validity conditions from simple mixed syllogisms apply.
3
Apply the standard conditions
The conditions (shurut) for validity of the syllogism composed of categorical and connected are those previously mentioned. Check if the reduced form satisfies them.
4
Derive the conclusion
The conclusion of the original mixed syllogism is the same as the conclusion of the reduced syllogism after substituting the appropriate terms.

Examples of Mixed Conditional Syllogisms with Reduction
Author provides two examples illustrating mixed conditional syllogisms with connected as minor and major.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to formalize the author's sample arguments using variable letters and determine the validity of the resulting compound syllogism.

Core Idea
The examples show how the conclusion becomes a conditional about possibility, not a categorical statement.

The author provides concrete examples with the connected as minor and as major. These illustrate the pattern and the resulting conclusion. By formalizing them, we can see the logical form clearly and verify validity. This skill directly applies to analyzing compound arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premises in the first example
Example with connected as minor: P1 (major) is a disjunctive exclusive of conjunction: 'Always either HZ or AB is exclusive of conjunction'. P2 (minor) is a connected conditional: 'Whenever CD is HZ, then HT'.
The connected is minor, so P2 is minor.
2
Determine the conclusion according to the author
The conclusion is: 'It may be that if always either CD or A-B is exclusive of conjunction, then HT'. This is a conditional with the possibility modal.
The conclusion replaces one disjunct (HZ) with the antecedent of the connected (CD).
3
Formalize the second example
Example with connected as major: P1 (major) is a connected: 'Whenever CD is HZ then HT'. P2 (minor) is a disjunctive exclusive of void: 'Not at all either HZ or AB is exclusive of void'. Conclusion: 'It may be that if not at all either CD or AB is exclusive of void, then HT'.
Similar pattern with swapped roles.
4
Check reduction to standard form
In both examples, the disjunctive premise is treated as the categorical. The connected premise remains. The conditions from the simple mixed syllogism should apply.
5
Validate the example
Verify that the reduction yields a valid syllogism: the categorical (disjunctive) and connected must satisfy the earlier conditions (e.g., the middle term must be distributed, etc.). The author implies validity.
The modal 'it may be' indicates the conclusion is not necessary but possible.

[B] As for the second of the two divisions, the connected therein is either affirmative or negative. In both cases, that conditional is either connected or disjunctive. In all cases, it is either the antecedent or the consequent of the connected. In all cases, the connected is either minor or major; these are sixteen cases, all of which reduce to the syllogism composed of categorical and connected, with the disjunctive taking the place of the categorical. The conditions are those mentioned earlier. The conclusions in them are the conclusions in what they reduce to, such that the disjunctive is placed in the syllogism in the place of the categorical there, and the result of composition of the two conditionals in the conclusion takes the place of the result of composition of the two categoricals there, as in our statement: The connected as minor: 'Whenever CD is HZ, then HT, and always either HZ or AB is exclusive of conjunction', it yields 'It may be that if always either CD or A-B is exclusive of conjunction, then HT'. And like our statement with the major and minor as they are: 'Not at all either HZ or AB is exclusive of void', it yields 'It may be that if not at all either CD or AB is exclusive of void, then HT'. This is when the connected is minor. As for when it is major, that is like our statement: 'Always either AB or CD is exclusive of void, and whenever CD is HZ then HT', it yields 'It may be that if either AB or HZ is exclusive of void, then HT'. And like our statement with the minor and major as they are: 'Not at all either AB or HZ is exclusive of conjunction' - HT. The explanation for all is what has been mentioned. And likewise for the remaining modes from each division; so consider what you have come to know, and with Allah is success.

Commentary
This passage covers the second division of the third main division, where the complete part is from the connected (muttasila) rather than the disjunctive. The author first enumerates the variables: the connected can be affirmative or negative (2 possibilities); the conditional (the incomplete part) can be connected or disjunctive (2); and it can be either the antecedent or the consequent of the connected (2); and the connected can be minor or major (2). This yields 2×2×2×2 = 16 cases. All reduce to a syllogism composed of a categorical and a connected, where the disjunctive takes the place of the categorical in the reduced form. The author then provides examples: one with the connected as minor, and one with it as major. In the first example, the connected is minor, and the major premise is a disjunctive exclusive of conjunction. The conclusion is a conditional statement expressing possibility. In the second example, the connected is major, and the minor premise is disjunctive. The author emphasizes that the reasoning is analogous to what was mentioned earlier. The passage concludes with an admonition to reflect on what has been learned.

The Syllogism

10 min
The Sixteen Types of Compound Syllogisms from Conditional and Disjunctive Premises
Author enumerates 16 types of compound syllogisms combining conditional and disjunctive premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate and construct the 16 types of compound syllogisms formed from conditional (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila) premises, and identify how they reduce to a basic form.

Core Idea
16 types arise from 4 binary choices; all reduce to a syllogism of categorical and conditional with a disjunctive in place of the categorical.

Compound syllogisms can combine conditional (if-then) and disjunctive (either-or) premises. The author systematically derives 16 distinct types by considering four binary variations: the quality of the conditional (affirmative/negative), whether it is conjunctive or disjunctive, whether it serves as antecedent or consequent, and whether it is minor or major. This lesson unpacks this derivation and shows the underlying pattern.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four binary variations
The passage states four variations: the conditional is either affirmative or negative; it is either conjunctive (muttasila) or disjunctive (munfasila); it is either the antecedent or the consequent of the compound; and it is either the minor or the major premise. Each variation gives two possibilities.
These variations are independent, leading to 2^4 = 16 types.
2
Understand how the types are derived
Starting from the second division (the conditional component), the author multiplies the possibilities: affirmative/negative × conjunctive/disjunctive × antecedent/consequent × minor/major. This systematic enumeration covers all compound syllogisms where one premise is conditional and the other is disjunctive.
The first division (earlier in the source) dealt with other combinations; this passage focuses on the second division.
3
Recognize the reduction to a basic syllogism
All 16 types reduce to a syllogism composed of categorical (hamliyya) and conditional (muttasila) premises, where the disjunctive takes the place of the categorical. The conclusion pattern mirrors that of categorical syllogisms but with conditionals.
This means the logical structure is analogous to standard categorical syllogisms, but with disjunctive propositions replacing categorical ones.
4
Examine an example with variables
The passage gives examples using variables A, B, J, D, H, Z, etc. For instance, with the conditional as minor: 'Whenever J D then H Z then H T' combined with 'Always either H Z or A B, incompatible with conjunction' yields 'It may be that if always either J D or B A, incompatible with conjunction, then H T.' Understand how the disjunctive condition (man'u al-jam') determines the conclusion.
The variables represent propositions. In real Islamic reasoning, these would be replaced with actual statements.
5
Apply the pattern to remaining moods
The author states that the same pattern applies to all remaining moods. Practice by creating a table of the 16 types, filling in one example for each combination. Use a simple Islamic topic like 'If the sun rises, then it is daytime' and 'Either it is day or night (but not both)'.
This helps solidify the systematic nature.

Commentary
This passage completes the discussion of compound syllogisms formed from conditional and disjunctive premises. The author first illustrates with a concluding example: a disjunctive premise (never is it either HZ or (if AB then JD)) combined with a conditional premise (whenever JD then HT) yields a disjunctive conclusion with conjunction-incompatibility. Then he turns to the second division of such compounds. Here, the conjunctive (conditional) component can be affirmative or negative, the overall conditional can be conjunctive or disjunctive, the conjunctive can be antecedent or consequent, and the conjunctive can be minor or major premise. Multiplying these options gives sixteen distinct types. The author explains that all these reduce to a syllogism composed of categorical and conjunctive premises, where the disjunctive takes the place of the categorical. The conditions and conclusions are analogous. He provides two detailed examples: one with the conjunctive as minor, another with it as major. In both, the disjunctive premise specifies either conjunction-incompatibility or disjunction-incompatibility (man'u al-jam' / man'u al-khuluw). The logical structure mirrors that of categorical syllogisms, but with conditionals. All examples use logical variables (أ، ب، ج، د، هـ، ز، ح، ط) representing terms or propositions. The passage concludes by noting that the same pattern applies to all remaining moods.

The Earlier Method of Deriving Categorical from Conditional Syllogisms
Earlier scholars' method of deriving categorical conclusions via reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the reductio proof used by earlier logicians to derive a categorical conclusion from conditional premises in the first figure, and identify the syllogistic figures involved.

Core Idea
Deriving a categorical from conditionals requires a reductio that assumes the contradictory and produces an impossibility.

In this lesson, we encounter an attempt by earlier logicians to derive categorical conclusions from purely conditional syllogisms. The method uses a complex interplay of third-figure reasoning and conversion, all aimed at proving a categorical proposition. We will walk through the first-figure example step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target conclusion and its contradictory.
The desired conclusion is 'Every J is A' (كل ج أ). Its contradictory is 'Some J is not A' (بعض ج ليس أ). This contradictory will be assumed for reductio.
2
Combine the contradictory with the minor premise in the third figure.
Take the minor premise 'Whenever every J is B, then HZ' and the contradictory. By the third figure, we derive a modal statement: 'It may be that if some B is not A, then HZ'.
The third figure here uses the minor premise as the major term.
3
Apply conversion to derive an impossibility.
Convert the modal statement to 'It may be that if HZ, then some B is not A'. This entails 'It may not be that if HZ, then every B is A', which contradicts the major premise 'Whenever HZ, then every B is A'.
The contradiction proves the original assumption false, so the desired categorical conclusion holds.
4
Observe the same pattern in the second figure example.
The second figure example uses a universal conditional and a negative conditional to derive 'No J is A' via an analogous reductio.
The pattern extends to third and fourth figures as noted.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [في المباحث المشتركة بين أقسام الأقيسة الشرطية ] وأما المباحث المشتركة بين الأقسام المذكورة من الأقيسة الشرطية فإنها متعددة غاية التعداد؛ منها ما ذهب إليه قوم من المتقدمين وهو الكلام في استنتاج الحملية من الأقيسة الشرطية والمختلطة من الشرطية والحملية. أما من الأقيسة الشرطية فالاشتراك من المقدمتين إما في جزء تام وذلك مثل قولنا في الشكل الأول كلما كان كل ج ب فـ ه ز، وكلما كان ه ز فكل ب أ ينتج كل ج أ وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا بعض ج ليس أ. والصغرى مع النقيض ينتج من الثالث قد يكون إذا كان بعض ب ليس أ فـ ه ز وإنه ينعكس إلى قولنا قد يكون إذا كان ه ز فبعض ب ليس أ. ويلزمه قد لا يكون إذا كان ه ز فكل ب أ، هذا خلف. ولأن قولنا قد يكون إذا كان بعض ب ليس أ فـ ه ز مع الكبرى ينتج من الشكل الأول قد يكون إذا كان بعض ب ليس أ فكل ب أ ؛ وإنه من جملة المستحيلات. وأما قولنا في الشكل الثاني فذلك كلما كان كل ج ب فـ ه ز، وليس البتة إذا كان بعض ب أ فـ ه ز ينتج لا شيء من ج أ وإلا لصدق نقيضه والصغرى مع النقيض ينتج من الثالث قد يكون إذا كان بعض ب أ فـ ه ز؛ وقد كان ليس البتة إذا كان بعض ب أ فـ ه ز، هذا خلف؛ وعلى هذا في الشكل الثالث والرابع ثم إنه وإن كان من جملة ما ذهب إليه قوم من المتقدمين، فلقائل أن يقول فيه : إنه في حيز المنع؛ إذ النتيجة الحملية لا تكون متحققة في نفس الأمر إلا وأن يتحقق من الأمور ما يحققه في نفس الأمر نحو المقدم من الصغرى أو غيره ؛ وذلك من جملة ما لا يُدرى حاله فلا يمكن أن يحكم بأنها متحققة في نفس الأمر بل يمكن أن تكون متحققة وأن لا تكون. ولا يقال كيف هو وقد كان من البراهين ما يبرهن به التحقق كما مر ؛ فإن ذلك وإن كان متحققا فلا
Chapter: On the common investigations among the divisions of conditional syllogisms. As for the common investigations among the mentioned divisions of conditional syllogisms, they are exceedingly numerous; among them is what some of the earlier scholars held, which is the discourse on deriving the categorical from conditional syllogisms and from mixed syllogisms of conditional and categorical. As for from conditional syllogisms: the overlap from the two premises is either in a complete part, such as our saying in the first figure: 'Whenever it is that every J is B, then H Z', and 'Whenever it is that H Z, then every B is A' produces 'Every J is A'; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our saying 'Some J is not A'. The minor premise with the contradictory produces from the third figure: 'It may be that if some B is not A, then H Z', and that converts to our saying 'It may be that if H Z, then some B is not A'. And it entails 'It may not be that if H Z, then every B is A'; this is a reductio. And because our saying 'It may be that if some B is not A, then H Z' with the major produces from the first figure 'It may be that if some B is not A, then every B is A'; and that is among the impossibilities. And as for our saying in the second figure: 'Whenever it is that every J is B, then H Z', and 'Never is it that if some B is A, then H Z' produces 'No J is A'; otherwise its contradictory would be true, and the minor with the contradictory produces from the third figure 'It may be that if some B is A, then H Z'; and it was 'Never is it that if some B is A, then H Z'; this is a reductio. And similarly in the third and fourth figures. Then, even though this is among what some earlier scholars held, someone may say about it: it is subject to denial; for the categorical conclusion is not actualized in itself unless there is actualized from the things what actualizes it in itself, like the major premise from the minor or other than it; and that is among the things whose state is unknown, so it cannot be judged that it is actualized in itself; rather it may be actualized or not. And it cannot be said 'How is that, when there are proofs that prove actualization as was mentioned?' For that, even if it is actualized, is not...
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage opens a chapter on common investigations among conditional syllogisms. The first topic is the attempt by some earlier logicians to derive categorical conclusions from purely conditional premises. The author illustrates with a first-figure example: 'Whenever every J is B, then HZ' and 'Whenever HZ, then every B is A' yield 'Every J is A'. To prove this, he uses a reductio: assume the contradictory (Some J is not A), combine it with the minor premise ('Whenever every J is B, then HZ') in the third figure, and derive a contradiction. The second figure example uses a universal conditional and a negative conditional to derive 'No J is A', again via reductio. The pattern extends to the third and fourth figures. However, the author then presents an objection: such derivations are subject to denial (man'). The categorical conclusion's actual truth depends on the actualization of certain conditions (e.g., the major premise from the minor), and since the truth of those conditions is unknown, we cannot assert the conclusion with certainty. The earlier proofs only show hypothetical implication, not actual truth. The critique reflects a deeper epistemological point: logical form alone cannot guarantee material truth.

استنتاج الحملية من الأقيسة الشرطية
خلف

Key Takeaway

The Syllogism
advanced
7 min
Critique of Deriving Categorical from Conditional Syllogisms
The author critiques the earlier method by denying that the categorical conclusion is actualized.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the epistemological objection to deriving categorical conclusions from conditional syllogisms: the truth of the conclusion depends on the actualization of premises, which is uncertain.

Core Idea
Logical form does not guarantee material truth; the categorical conclusion's actualization remains unknown.

Despite the formal validity of the derivation presented earlier, the author raises a powerful objection: the categorical conclusion is only actual if the premises are actualized in reality, and that actualization is not guaranteed by the conditional form itself. This critique highlights the distinction between hypothetical and categorical truth.

Guided Path
1
State the claimed inference.
The earlier logicians claimed that from conditional premises, a categorical conclusion follows necessarily (e.g., from two conditionals, we get 'Every J is A').
2
Identify the condition for actual truth.
The author argues that for the categorical conclusion to be actually true in reality (tahaqquq), certain conditions must be actualized, such as the truth of the minor premise's antecedent or other external factors.
The conditional premises themselves do not guarantee that their antecedents are actually true.
3
Explain why those conditions are unknown.
The actualization of the conditions is 'among the things whose state is unknown', so we cannot be certain whether the conclusion holds in reality.
Thus the derivation does not yield certain knowledge (yaqin).
4
Assess the strength of the objection.
The objection does not claim the derivation is invalid in form, but that it is insufficient for asserting a categorical truth in the actual world. This aligns with the distinction between formal validity and material truth.
Later logicians generally accept this restriction, limiting the method to hypothetical contexts.
