Second Division of Compound Conditionals: One Shared Part
Explains the four sub-cases when one premise shares one part with the other in compound conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four sub-divisions of compound conditionals where one premise shares one part with the other, and construct the resulting compound conditional conclusion for each.

Core Idea
When one premise shares a single part with another, the compound yields a conditional linking their conclusions.

Compound conditionals are central to complex arguments in Islamic logic. The author here classifies a special case: when two conditional premises share exactly one part (either the antecedent or consequent). Understanding these four patterns — based on which premise is minor/major and which part is shared — enables you to systematically derive new implications. This lesson unpacks each sub-division with concrete variable-based examples and shows how the proof relies on the third figure of categorical syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shared part and premise roles
Determine whether the conditional that shares both parts (the 'sharing conditional') is the minor or major premise. Then identify whether the shared part from the other premise is its antecedent or consequent. This yields four mutually exclusive cases.
The author lists these as: (1) minor shares both, major's antecedent shared; (2) minor shares both, major's consequent shared; (3) major shares both, minor's antecedent shared; (4) major shares both, minor's consequent shared.
2
Extract the two simple syllogisms
For each case, write the two conditionals explicitly using the variables (ج, ب, أ, ط, هـ, ز). For example, in case 1: minor = 'Whenever every J is B, then every B is T'; major = 'Whenever every J is B, then H is Z'.
These are the premises that will be reduced to simple syllogisms.
3
Derive the simple conclusion (bi-i'tibār al-basāṭa)
Apply the third figure of categorical syllogisms to each simple syllogism to obtain an intermediate conclusion. The author states the proof is from the third figure, meaning the middle term is the subject of both premises.
The intermediate conclusions are particular affirmative propositions, e.g., 'Some T is J' and 'H is Z' from the example.
4
Form the compound conditional
Combine the two intermediate conclusions into a new conditional: the antecedent is the implication of one conclusion for the other (mulāzama). The author gives the resulting conditional for each case, e.g., 'It may be that whenever every J is B, then some T is J' and 'Whenever every J is B, then H is Z'.
The compound conditional is typically affirmative in the first three sub-divisions; the fourth is also a conditional.
5
Verify the proof from the third figure
Check that the term arrangement matches the third figure: the middle term (the shared part) is subject in both premises, and the conclusion follows by conversion or immediate inference. The author affirms this for all four sub-divisions.
This step reinforces the connection to earlier lessons on categorical syllogisms.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثاني وهو الذي يشارك أحد طرفي إحدى المقدمتين كل واحد من طرفي الأخرى فلا بد من كون التأليفين فيه على شكلين. وينتج نتيجة كل واحد من التأليفين باعتبار البساطة، وباعتبار التركيب متصلة أخرى أحد طرفيها ملازمة نتيجة أحد التأليفين لنتيجة التأليف الآخر. وتفصيل القول في هذا القسم أن المتصلة المشاركة الجزأين إما أن تكون الصغرى هي أو الكبرى. وعلى التقديرين فإما أن يكون المشارك من المقدمة الأخرى مقدمها أو تاليها؛ فهذه أربعة أقسام ينتج كل قسم منها باعتبار بساطته كما مر، وباعتبار التركيب كذلك زائدة على النتيجتين. وإنها في القسم الأول متّصلة موجبة كما في قولنا كلما كان كل «ج ب» فكل ب ط»، وكلما كان كل «ج ب» فـ «هز» ينتج قد يكون إذا كان كلما كان كل ج ب» فبعــض «طج»، وكلما كان كل ج ب» فـ هـ ز»؛ والبرهان من الثالث على ما عرف من قبل. وفي القسم الثاني متصلة موجبة أيضًا كما في قولنا كلما كان كل «ج ب» فـكـل «بط»، وكلَّما كان «هــز» فكل ج ب ينتج قد يكون إذا كان كلما كان كل «ج ب» فبعض طج) فقد يكون إذا كان هـز» فكل ج) ب؛ والبرهان من الثالث أيضًا. ج» وفي القسم الثالث متصلة موجبة كذلك كما في قولنا كلما كان كل «ج ب» فـ «هز»، وكلما كان كل «ب أ» فكل ج ز » ينتج قد يكون إذا كان كلَّما كان كل ج أ» فـ «هز»، وكلما كان كل «ج أ» فبعض «بج»؛ والبرهان من الثالث. وفي القسم الرابع / متّصلة أيضًا كما في قولنا كلما كان «هز» فكل فكل «جب»، وكلما كان كل ب أ) فكل ج ز ينتج قد يكون إذا كان كلما كان «هز» فكل ج أ» فقد يكون إذا كان كل «ج أ» فبعض «ب ج»؛ والبرهان من الثالث كذلك. وأما المتّصلة الجزئية المؤلفة من نتيجتي التأليفين كما في القسم الأول من أصل الأقسام الأربعة فإنها من جملة ما ينتجه القسم الثاني والرابع من أقسام هذا القسم: وهما اللذان المشارك من المقدّمة المشاركة الجزء الواحد منهما تاليها والمتّصلة المؤلفة من نتيجة القياس بحسب البساطة التي مرَّ ذكرها في القسم الأول؛ فلازمه لكل واحد من أقسام هذا القسم لما مر .
As for the second division, which is where one of the two ends of one of the two premises shares with each one of the two ends of the other [premise], it is necessary that the two compositions be in two forms. And it produces the conclusion of each of the two compositions in terms of simplicity, and in terms of composition [it produces] another conditional whose one end is the implication of the conclusion of one of the two compositions for the conclusion of the other composition. The detailed statement about this division is that the conditional that shares the two parts is either the minor or the major. And in both cases, the shared [part] from the other premise is either its antecedent or its consequent; these are four divisions. Each division produces in terms of its simplicity as before, and in terms of composition likewise additional to the two conclusions. And they are in the first division an affirmative conditional, as in our saying: 'Whenever every J is B, then every B is T', and 'Whenever every J is B, then H is Z'. It produces: 'It may be that whenever every J is B, then some T is J', and 'Whenever every J is B, then H is Z'; and the proof from the third [figure] as known before. And in the second division an affirmative conditional as well, as in our saying: 'Whenever every J is B, then every B is T', and 'Whenever H is Z, then every J is B'. It produces: 'It may be that whenever every J is B, then some T is J', then 'It may be that whenever H is Z, then every J is B'; and the proof from the third [figure] as well. And in the third division an affirmative conditional likewise, as in our saying: 'Whenever every J is B, then H is Z', and 'Whenever every B is A, then every J is Z'. It produces: 'It may be that whenever every J is A, then H is Z', and 'Whenever every J is A, then some B is J'; and the proof from the third [figure]. And in the fourth division a conditional as well, as in our saying: 'Whenever H is Z, then every J is B', and 'Whenever every B is A, then every J is Z'. It produces: 'It may be that whenever H is Z, then every J is A', then 'It may be that whenever every J is A, then some B is J'; and the proof from the third [figure] likewise. As for the particular conditional composed of the two conclusions of the two compositions, as in the first division from the root of the four divisions, it is among what the second and fourth divisions of this section produce: they are the two [divisions] where the shared [part] from the premise sharing the one part is its consequent, and the conditional composed of the conclusion of the syllogism in terms of simplicity that was mentioned in the first division; so its implication for each one of the divisions of this section [is] as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 226

Commentary
This passage discusses the second division of compound conditional syllogisms (qiyas murakkab) where one of the two premises shares exactly one part (either the antecedent or consequent) with the other premise. The author first states the general rule: such a composition must take place in two forms (i.e., two separate syllogisms) that each produce a conclusion in terms of simplicity, and when combined, they yield another conditional proposition whose antecedent is the implication of one conclusion for the other. He then elaborates: the conditional that shares the two parts can be either the minor or the major premise. In both cases, the part shared from the other premise can be either its antecedent or its consequent, giving four sub-divisions. For each, he gives an example using variables (ج, ب, أ, etc.). For the first sub-division, the minor premise shares both parts; the shared part from the major is its antecedent. The result is an affirmative conditional. The proof is from the third figure (of categorical syllogisms), presumably referring to the arrangement of terms. The author similarly describes the second, third, and fourth sub-divisions, each yielding an affirmative conditional (except the fourth which is simply a conditional) and provides examples. Finally, he notes that the particular conditional composed of the two conclusions (from the simple syllogisms) is produced by the second and fourth sub-divisions of this section, and that this conclusion implies the same for each sub-division as previously established.

The Implications and Entailments of the Third Division of Compound Conditionals
Describes the results of the third division in two sub-categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the three implications and four entailments produced by the third division of compound conditionals.

Core Idea
The third division yields three implications from one perspective and four entailments from another.

Having understood the condition for the third division, we now explore the results it produces. The author explains that from the perspective of the first sub-division, three implications (ملازمة) arise, while from the second sub-division, four entailments (استلزام) emerge. Understanding these results is crucial for applying compound conditionals in Islamic logical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sub-divisions
The passage divides the results of the third division into two sub-divisions: one that yields three results and another that yields four results. The first sub-division is described as 'the first division of the division we are in', and the second as 'the four divisions that are the second division'.
These sub-divisions correspond to different levels of compositeness.
2
List the three implications from the first sub-division
The author states three implications: (1) the implication of the conclusion of the composition between the two consequents for that of the composition between the two antecedents; (2) the implication of the conclusion of the composition between the minor's consequent and the major's antecedent for that of the composition between the minor's antecedent and the major's consequent; and (3) the reverse of (2).
These are relations between complex conclusions.
3
List the four entailments from the second sub-division
From the second sub-division, four entailments (استلزام) are produced, each being an implication of one implication for another. The author enumerates them, though the text is cut off.
The fourth entailment is partially given.
4
Contrast implication (ملازمة) and entailment (استلزام)
The term mulāzama refers to an implication between two statements, while istilzām is a higher-order entailment: one implication necessarily entails another implication. This distinction is key to understanding the layers of logical relations.
In later works, these terms may be used interchangeably.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الثالث وهو الذي كل واحد من طرفي إحدى المقدمتين لكل واحد من طرفي الأخرى فإنه يشتمل على التأليفات الأربعة وهي التي في الأربعة من المتصلتين المشتركتين في جزء غير تام منهما؛ وينتج باعتبار البساطة النتائج الأربعَ التي مرَّ ذكرها في تلك الأقسام الأربعة، وباعتبار اشتماله على القسمين اللذين هما :قسمان: القسم الأول من القسم الذي نحن فيه ثلاث نتائج إحداها ملازمة نتيجة التأليف بين التاليين لنتيجة التأليف بين المقدمين. والثانية ملازمة نتيجة التأليف بين تالي الصغرى ومقدم الكبرى لنتيجة التأليف بين مقدم الصغرى وتالي الكبرى. والثالثة ملازمة نتيجة التأليف بين مقدم الصغرى وتالي الكبرى لنتيجة التأليف بين تالي الصغرى ومقدم الكبرى وباعتبار اشتماله على الأقسام الأربعة التي هي القسم الثاني ينتج النتائج الأربع التي هي نتائج تلك الأربعة: الأولى استلزام ملازمة نتيجة التأليف بين تالي الصغرى ومقدم الكبرى لنتيجة التأليف بين المقدمتين لملازمة تالي الكبرى لنتيجة التأليف بين المقدمتين. والثانية استلزام ملازمة نتيجة التأليف بين التاليين لنتيجة التأليف بين تالي الكبرى ومقدم الصغرى لملازمة نتيجة التأليف بين تالي الكبرى ومقدم الصغرى لمقدم الكبرى والثالثة استلزام ملازمة تالي الصغرى لنتيجة التأليف بين المقدمتين لملازمة نتيجة التأليف بين التاليين لنتيجة التأليف بين المقدمتين. والرابعة استلزام ملازمة نتيجة التأليف بين تالي الصغرى ومقدم الكبرى لمقدم الصغرى لملازمة نتيجة التأليف بين التاليين لنتيجة التأليف بين تالي الصغرى ومقدم الكبرى؛ والبيان في الكل ما
As for the third division, which is where each one of the two ends of one of the two premises [corresponds to] each one of the two ends of the other, it includes the four compositions which are those in the four [cases] of the two conditionals that share an incomplete part of them; and it produces, in terms of simplicity, the four conclusions that were mentioned in those four divisions, and in terms of its inclusion of the two divisions that are: the first division of the division we are in yields three results: one of them is the implication of the conclusion of the composition between the two consequents for the conclusion of the composition between the two antecedents. The second is the implication of the conclusion of the composition between the consequent of the minor and the antecedent of the major for the conclusion of the composition between the antecedent of the minor and the consequent of the major. The third is the implication of the conclusion of the composition between the antecedent of the minor and the consequent of the major for the conclusion of the composition between the consequent of the minor and the antecedent of the major. And in terms of its inclusion of the four divisions that are the second division, it produces the four results that are the results of those four: the first is the entailment of the implication of the conclusion of the composition between the consequent of the minor and the antecedent of the major for the conclusion of the composition between the two premises to the implication of the consequent of the major for the conclusion of the composition between the two premises. The second is the entailment of the implication of the conclusion of the composition between the two consequents for the conclusion of the composition between the consequent of the major and the antecedent of the minor to the implication of the conclusion of the composition between the consequent of the major and the antecedent of the minor for the antecedent of the major. The third is the entailment of the implication of the consequent of the minor for the conclusion of the composition between the two premises to the implication of the conclusion of the composition between the two consequents for the conclusion of the composition between the two premises. The fourth is the entailment of the implication of the conclusion of the composition between the consequent of the minor and the antecedent of the major for the antecedent of the minor to the implication of the conclusion of the composition between the two consequents for the conclusion of the composition between the consequent of the minor and the antecedent of the major; and the proof for all is...
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This passage presents the third division of compound conditional syllogisms, where each of the two ends (antecedent and consequent) of one premise corresponds to each end of the other premise. This yields four compound syllogisms (التأليفات الأربعة) built from two conditionals that share an incomplete part (i.e., one term from each). In terms of simplicity, each of the four simple syllogisms yields one of the four conclusions previously mentioned. Then, considering the compositeness, the author explains two sub-cases. First, when the division encompasses the first sub-division of this section, it produces three implications: (1) the implication of the conclusion of the composition between the two consequents for that of the composition between the two antecedents; (2) the implication of the conclusion of the composition between the minor's consequent and the major's antecedent for that of the composition between the minor's antecedent and the major's consequent; (3) the reverse of (2). Second, when it encompasses the second sub-division (the four divisions), it produces four results, each being an entailment (استلزام) of one implication for another. The author lists these four entailments, but the passage ends abruptly with 'والبيان في الكل ما' (and the proof for all is...), likely cut off by the page break.

?
Task 2: From the same premises, attempt to construct the four entailments from the second sub-division. If the text is incomplete, describe what the pattern might be and what the fourth entailment could be.

The Four Basic Compositions of the Third Conditional Syllogism
Author lists the four compositions of the third type.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four basic compositions from two conjunctive conditional premises sharing a partial common term.

Core Idea
The third type yields four basic compositions, analogous to the four figures of categorical syllogisms.

When two conjunctive conditionals share a common term, they can be composed in four distinct ways. The author calls these the four compositions. Understanding them is key to deriving valid conclusions from compound conditionals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditional premises
Both premises are conjunctive conditionals (متصلة). In the example, the first is 'Whenever every A is B, then every C is D' and the second is 'Whenever every A is C, then none of B is D'.
Read the premises carefully: they have the form 'If P then Q'.
2
Discover the shared partial term
The term 'أ' (A) appears in the antecedent of both premises. It is the partial common part referred to by the author.
The shared term may be in antecedents, consequents, or crossed.
3
List the four compositions
The author states there are four compositions. They involve pairing the antecedents and consequents: (1) antecedents with antecedents, (2) consequents with consequents, (3) antecedent of first with consequent of second, (4) consequent of first with antecedent of second.
These are the four ways to combine the terms.
4
Recognize the four conclusions
Each composition yields a conclusion that is a necessary consequence (ملازمة). These conclusions are analogous to the four figures of categorical syllogisms, as mentioned in earlier sections.
The author says they are the same as those previously mentioned.

As for the third section, which is that each one of the two sides of one premise corresponds to each one of the two sides of the other premise, it comprises the four compositions, which are those in the four [compositions] of the two conjunctive conditionals sharing an incomplete part from them. And the third is the necessary consequence of the conclusion of the composition between the antecedent of the minor and the consequent of the major of the conclusion of the composition between the consequent of the minor and the antecedent of the major. And the demonstration in all is as previously: an example is our saying: 'Whenever every A is B, then every C is D, and whenever every A is C, then none of B is D.' And this example comprises four compositions, each of them from every figure, so it yields the four conclusions according to simplicity. So the conclusions that were mentioned also according to composition, and this is apparent, known by reflection, if God wills.

Commentary
This passage explains the third type of conditional syllogistic composition, where each term (side) of one premise relates to each term of the other premise. The author describes four basic compositions that arise from two conjunctive conditionals sharing a partial common part. He then enumerates the conclusions from two perspectives: simplicity (basic syllogistic forms) and composition (compound forms). First, from the perspective of simplicity, the third type yields the same four conclusions already mentioned for the previous four divisions. Then, considering its inclusion of two sub-sections, he lists three additional necessary consequence relations. Finally, considering its inclusion of four sub-sections (the second section), he lists four more implication relations. The author provides an example with letters A, B, C, D: 'Whenever every A is B, then every C is D, and whenever every A is C, then none of B is D.' This example contains four compositions, each from a specific figure, yielding four conclusions. The overall structure is complex but demonstrates the systematic approach to enumerating all possible syllogistic forms and their results. The author concludes that this is clear through reflection.

Key Terms
التأليف (al-ta'līf): Composition of two conditional premises into a syllogistic form; here it refers to the specific pairing of antecedents and consequents.
ملازمة (mulāzamah): Necessary consequence or implication; a relation between two propositions such that one entails the other.
بساطة (basāṭah): Simplicity; the basic elementary syllogistic form without additional combination.
تركيب (tarkīb): Composition; combining simple syllogisms to form more complex ones.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Four
C) Three
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The third conditional syllogism always yields four basic compositions, which are the foundation for all derivations.

Compound Conclusions from the Third Conditional Syllogism
Author enumerates the compound conclusions from the two sub-sections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the three and four compound conclusions described in the third type of conditional syllogism.

Core Idea
Beyond the four basic conclusions, the third type yields nested implications called ملازمة and استلزام.

After establishing the four basic compositions, the author delves into more complex conclusions that involve necessary consequences between the results of simpler compositions. This lesson unpacks those compound conclusions.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four basic compositions
The four basic compositions are the foundation. They produce four simple conclusions. Now we move to compound conclusions that combine these.
Basic compositions are the building blocks.
2
Understand the two sub-sections
The author divides the third type into two sub-sections: the first yields three conclusions, the second yields four. These are compound conclusions (ملازمة and استلزام).
These are not additional independent conclusions but relations between simpler ones.
3
Analyze the three conclusions of the first sub-section
The first conclusion states that the ملازمة of the composition of the two consequents implies the ملازمة of the composition of the two antecedents. The second and third involve cross compositions.
Each conclusion is a ملازمة between two ملازمة statements.
4
Analyze the four conclusions of the second sub-section
The second sub-section yields four 'استلزام' (implications) between ملازمة statements. For example, the first is that the ملازمة of the composition of consequent of minor and antecedent of major (of the conclusion of composition of the two premises) implies the ملازمة of the consequent of major (of that same conclusion).
These are even more nested.
5
Use the example to verify one compound conclusion
Take the example with A, B, C, D and try to see if one of the compound implications holds. This requires working through the compositions step by step.
This is a deep exercise in symbolic logic.

The Fourth Type of Conditional Syllogism: Structure and Subdivisions
Author describes the fourth type of conditional syllogism with three compositions and four subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the pattern of the fourth type of conditional composition and enumerate its four subdivisions.

Core Idea
One part shares both, the other shares one—four subdivisions based on which parts share.

This lesson unpacks the structure of the fourth type of conditional syllogistic composition. You will learn how to recognize the key sharing relationships and the four possible subdivisions that arise from the arrangement of antecedents and consequents. This pattern is foundational for understanding complex arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the sharing pattern
In the fourth type, one part (antecedent or consequent) of one premise shares each part of the other premise, while the other part shares only one part of the other premise.
This creates a specific relationship where one term appears in both parts of the other premise.
2
Visualize the three compositions
The sharing pattern gives rise to three distinct compositions: the direct combination of both premises, and two cross-compositions between the parts.
The author does not list them explicitly here but mentions they exist.
3
Note the diversity of figures
These compositions do not all fall under a single figure; they occur in different figures, meaning the arrangement of parts varies.
Figure refers to the arrangement of terms in a syllogism.
4
Enumerate the four subdivisions
The subdivisions depend on which part (antecedent or consequent) of the major premise shares both parts of the minor, and which part of the minor shares both parts of the major. The author lists four possibilities.
The first subdivision is illustrated with an example in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الرابع وهو الذي يشارك أحد طرفي إحدى المقدمتين كل واحد من طرفي الأخرى والطرف الآخر منهما لأحد طرفي الأخرى فقط؛ وإنه يشتمل على تأليفات ثلاثة. ولا تقع هذه التأليفات على شكل واحد. ويلزم بالضرورة أن يكون في كل واحد من المقدمتين جزء مشارك لكل واحد من جزأي الأخرى، وجزء مشارك لأحد جزأي الأخرى فقط. وأقسامه أربعة؛ إذ الجزء المشارك لكل واحد من جزأي الكبرى إما أن يكون مقدمها أو تاليها. وعلى التقديرين فالمشارك من الكبرى لكل واحد من جزأي الصغرى إما مقدمها، أو تاليها . فالقسم الأول مثل قولنا كلَّما كان كل «جب» فلا شيء من «ط أ»، وكلما كان كل «ب أ» فكل «ده» ينتج باعتبار البساطة ثلاث نتائج؛ نتيجة التأليف بين المقدمتين، ونتيجة التأليف بين مقدم الصغرى وتالي الكبرى، ونتيجة التأليف بين تالي الصغرى ومقدم الكبرى. وباعتبار اشتماله على التأليفين أعني التأليف بين تالي الصغرى ومقدم الكبرى، والتأليف بين مقدم الصغرى وتالي الكبرى ينتج القسم الثاني من القسم الأول. وباعتبار اشتماله على التأليف بين مقدم الكبرى وكل واحد من جزأي الصغرى ينتج القسم الثاني من القسم الثاني. وباعتبار اشتماله على التأليف بين مقدم الصغرى وكل واحد من جزأي الصغرى ينتج القسم الثاني من القسم الثاني. وينتج أيضًا استلزام كل واحدة من هذه النتائج الخمس لكل واحد من الأربع الباقية.
As for the fourth section, which is that one of the two sides of one premise shares each one of the two sides of the other premise, and the other side of it shares only one of the two sides of the other premise; and it comprises three compositions. And these compositions do not occur in a single figure. It necessarily follows that in each of the two premises there is a part that shares each of the two parts of the other premise, and a part that shares only one of the two parts of the other premise. And its subdivisions are four: for the part that shares each of the two parts of the major is either its antecedent or its consequent. And on both suppositions, the part from the major that shares each of the two parts of the minor is either its antecedent or its consequent. So the first subdivision is like our saying: 'Whenever every J is B, then none of T is A, and whenever every B is A, then every D is H' — it yields, with respect to simplicity, three conclusions: the conclusion of the composition between the two premises, the conclusion of the composition between the antecedent of the minor and the consequent of the major, and the conclusion of the composition between the consequent of the minor and the antecedent of the major. And with respect to its including the two compositions, namely the composition between the consequent of the minor and the antecedent of the major, and the composition between the antecedent of the minor and the consequent of the major, it yields the second section of the first section. And with respect to its including the composition between the antecedent of the major and each of the two parts of the minor, it yields the second section of the second section. And with respect to its including the composition between the antecedent of the minor and each of the two parts of the minor, it yields the second section of the second section. And it also yields the implication of each of these five conclusions for each of the remaining four.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage explains the fourth type of conditional syllogistic composition, where one part of one premise relates to both parts of the other premise, and the other part relates to only one part. It comprises three compositions, which do not all belong to a single figure (i.e., they appear in different figures). The author then subdivides this type into four sub-sections based on which part of the major (antecedent or consequent) corresponds to both parts of the minor, and which part of the minor corresponds to both parts of the major. As an example, he gives: 'Whenever every J is B, then none of T is A, and whenever every B is A, then every D is H.' From this example, considering simplicity, three conclusions are obtained: (1) the composition of both premises directly, (2) composition of the antecedent of the minor with the consequent of the major, and (3) composition of the consequent of the minor with the antecedent of the major. Then, by considering inclusion of certain compositions, it yields results from the second section of the first and second sections respectively. Finally, he notes that each of these five conclusions implies each of the remaining four, indicating a network of necessary consequences.

Key Terms
مشارك
الشكل
الجزء
استلزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Four

Key Takeaway
The fourth type of conditional syllogism is defined by a specific sharing pattern, leading to three compositions and four subdivisions.

(Hint: Check if one part of one premise shares both parts of the other.)

Deriving Conclusions from the Example in the Fourth Type
Author provides an example and enumerates five conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the five conclusions from the example provided in the fourth type of conditional composition.

Core Idea
Two conditionals with shared terms yield multiple conclusions by composing different parts.

The author illustrates the fourth type with an example using variables. This lesson walks you through the process of extracting the five conclusions—three from simple composition and two from specific compositions—and understanding how they imply each other. This ability is crucial for handling complex conditional arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditionals
The example is: 'Whenever every J is B, then none of T is A' and 'Whenever every B is A, then every D is H'. Recognize the major and minor premises.
The first premise is treated as the minor, the second as the major.
2
Recognize the shared term
The term 'ب' appears in both antecedents. It is the term that shares with both parts of the other premise? Actually the pattern is more complex; focus on the composability.
The sharing pattern determines which compositions are possible.
3
List the three simple conclusions
The simple conclusions are: (1) composition of the two full premises, (2) antecedent of minor with consequent of major, (3) consequent of minor with antecedent of major.
These are derived by considering the premises as they are.
4
Identify the additional compositions
By including specific combinations, two more conclusions emerge: the second section of the first section and the second section of the second section. This brings the total to five distinct conclusions.
The author mentions these as resulting from including the two cross-compositions and the composition of the major antecedent with both parts of the minor.
5
Note the network of implications
Each of the five conclusions implies each of the remaining four, forming a dense logical structure.
This 'istilzām' shows the richness of conditional compositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الرابع وهو الذي يشارك أحد طرفي إحدى المقدمتين كل واحد من طرفي الأخرى والطرف الآخر منهما لأحد طرفي الأخرى فقط؛ وإنه يشتمل على تأليفات ثلاثة. ولا تقع هذه التأليفات على شكل واحد. ويلزم بالضرورة أن يكون في كل واحد من المقدمتين جزء مشارك لكل واحد من جزأي الأخرى، وجزء مشارك لأحد جزأي الأخرى فقط. وأقسامه أربعة؛ إذ الجزء المشارك لكل واحد من جزأي الكبرى إما أن يكون مقدمها أو تاليها. وعلى التقديرين فالمشارك من الكبرى لكل واحد من جزأي الصغرى إما مقدمها، أو تاليها . فالقسم الأول مثل قولنا كلَّما كان كل «جب» فلا شيء من «ط أ»، وكلما كان كل «ب أ» فكل «ده» ينتج باعتبار البساطة ثلاث نتائج؛ نتيجة التأليف بين المقدمتين، ونتيجة التأليف بين مقدم الصغرى وتالي الكبرى، ونتيجة التأليف بين تالي الصغرى ومقدم الكبرى. وباعتبار اشتماله على التأليفين أعني التأليف بين تالي الصغرى ومقدم الكبرى، والتأليف بين مقدم الصغرى وتالي الكبرى ينتج القسم الثاني من القسم الأول. وباعتبار اشتماله على التأليف بين مقدم الكبرى وكل واحد من جزأي الصغرى ينتج القسم الثاني من القسم الثاني. وباعتبار اشتماله على التأليف بين مقدم الصغرى وكل واحد من جزأي الصغرى ينتج القسم الثاني من القسم الثاني. وينتج أيضًا استلزام كل واحدة من هذه النتائج الخمس لكل واحد من الأربع الباقية.
As for the fourth section, which is that one of the two sides of one premise shares each one of the two sides of the other premise, and the other side of it shares only one of the two sides of the other premise; and it comprises three compositions. And these compositions do not occur in a single figure. It necessarily follows that in each of the two premises there is a part that shares each of the two parts of the other premise, and a part that shares only one of the two parts of the other premise. And its subdivisions are four: for the part that shares each of the two parts of the major is either its antecedent or its consequent. And on both suppositions, the part from the major that shares each of the two parts of the minor is either its antecedent or its consequent. So the first subdivision is like our saying: 'Whenever every J is B, then none of T is A, and whenever every B is A, then every D is H' — it yields, with respect to simplicity, three conclusions: the conclusion of the composition between the two premises, the conclusion of the composition between the antecedent of the minor and the consequent of the major, and the conclusion of the composition between the consequent of the minor and the antecedent of the major. And with respect to its including the two compositions, namely the composition between the consequent of the minor and the antecedent of the major, and the composition between the antecedent of the minor and the consequent of the major, it yields the second section of the first section. And with respect to its including the composition between the antecedent of the major and each of the two parts of the minor, it yields the second section of the second section. And with respect to its including the composition between the antecedent of the minor and each of the two parts of the minor, it yields the second section of the second section. And it also yields the implication of each of these five conclusions for each of the remaining four.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage explains the fourth type of conditional syllogistic composition, where one part of one premise relates to both parts of the other premise, and the other part relates to only one part. It comprises three compositions, which do not all belong to a single figure (i.e., they appear in different figures). The author then subdivides this type into four sub-sections based on which part of the major (antecedent or consequent) corresponds to both parts of the minor, and which part of the minor corresponds to both parts of the major. As an example, he gives: 'Whenever every J is B, then none of T is A, and whenever every B is A, then every D is H.' From this example, considering simplicity, three conclusions are obtained: (1) the composition of both premises directly, (2) composition of the antecedent of the minor with the consequent of the major, and (3) composition of the consequent of the minor with the antecedent of the major. Then, by considering inclusion of certain compositions, it yields results from the second section of the first and second sections respectively. Finally, he notes that each of these five conclusions implies each of the remaining four, indicating a network of necessary consequences.

The Fourth Division of Compound Syllogisms
Author defines the fourth division of compound syllogisms and its four subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth division of compound syllogisms by its participation pattern and enumerate its four subdivisions.

Core Idea
The fourth division: one term shares with both, the other with only one.

Compound syllogisms are often classified by how their premises share terms. The fourth division has a distinctive pattern: one term from each premise participates with both terms of the other premise, while the other term participates with only one. This classification helps logicians analyze complex arguments. Let's unpack this definition step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The passage defines 'the fourth division' (al-qism al-rabi') of compound syllogisms. Notice it is a category within a broader system of classification.
The term 'compound syllogisms' refers to syllogisms with more than two premises.
2
Extract the defining characteristic
The key feature: one term of one premise participates with both terms of the other premise, while the other term participates with only one. This pattern is unique to this division.
Participation means the term appears in both premises, serving as a link.
3
Note the necessary conditions
The author states it necessarily contains three compositions (ta'lifat) and does not fall under a single figure. This means the three possible arrangements of premises do not all share the same syllogistic figure.
A composition is a specific arrangement of premises in a syllogism.
4
Recognize the four subdivisions
The subdivisions arise based on whether the part that participates with each part of the other premise is the antecedent or consequent in both premises. This yields four possibilities.
This is a logical classification, not an empirical one. Understanding it aids in analyzing complex arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القسم الرابع وهو الذي يشارك أحد طرفي إحدى المقدمتين كل واحد من طرفي الأخرى والطرف الآخر منهما لأحد طرفي الأخرى فقط؛ وإنه يشتمل على تأليفات ثلاثة. ولا تقع هذه التأليفات على شكل واحد. ويلزم بالضرورة أن يكون في كل واحد من المقدمتين جزء مشارك لكل واحد من جزأي الأخرى، وجزء مشارك لأحد جزأي الأخرى فقط. وأقسامه أربعة؛ إذ الجزء المشارك لكل واحد من جزأي الكبرى إما أن يكون مقدمها أو تاليها. وعلى التقديرين فالمشارك من الكبرى لكل واحد من جزأي الصغرى إما مقدمها، أو تاليها.
As for the fourth division, it is that in which one of the two terms of one premise participates with each of the two terms of the other premise, and the other term of those two participates with only one of the two terms of the other. It comprises three compositions. These compositions do not fall under a single figure. It necessarily follows that in each of the two premises there is a part that participates with each of the two parts of the other, and a part that participates with only one of the two parts of the other. Its divisions are four: for the part that participates with each of the two parts of the major premise is either its antecedent or its consequent. On both suppositions, the part from the major that participates with each of the two parts of the minor is either its antecedent or its consequent.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage defines the fourth division of compound syllogisms. The author explains that in this type, one term from one premise shares with both terms of the other premise, while the other term from the first premise shares with only one term of the other. This division necessarily contains three compositions, and these compositions do not all fit into a single figure of the syllogism. The author then clarifies that each premise must have a part that participates with each part of the other premise, and a part that participates with only one part of the other. From this, four subdivisions arise based on whether the participating part in the major premise is its antecedent or consequent, and similarly for the minor premise.

Key Terms
القسم الرابع (qism rabi')
تأليف (ta'lif)
مقدمة (muqaddima)
مقدم (muqaddam)
تالي (tali)

Key Takeaway
The fourth division of compound syllogisms is defined by a participation pattern where one term shares with both and the other with only one, leading to four subdivisions based on antecedent/consequent positions.

Furthermore, if it has a cause, that cause is eternal. Therefore, the cause of the world is eternal.' First Subdivision of the Fourth Division with Example
Author gives an example of the first subdivision and lists the resulting conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three simple and five total results from the first subdivision of the fourth division in compound conditional syllogisms.

Core Idea
Compound conditional syllogisms yield multiple results through simple and composite combinations.

This lesson walks through an example of the first subdivision of the fourth division. You will see how from two conditional premises, multiple derived conditionals are produced through both simple and composite compositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conditional premises
Find the two conditional statements in the example: 'Whenever every B is C, then no T is A' and 'Whenever every A is B, then every D is E'.
Note the form: each is a conditional with antecedent and consequent.
2
Understand simplicity vs. composition
Simplicity considers only the direct composition of the two full premises. Composition involves combining parts (antecedents and consequents) from each premise.
The text uses 'basata' for simplicity and 'tarkib' for composition.
3
List the three simple results
According to simplicity, we get: (1) the composition of the two full premises, (2) the composition of the minor's antecedent with the major's consequent, and (3) the composition of the minor's consequent with the major's antecedent.
These are the three 'results' from the direct combination of the premises.
4
Recognize additional composite results
By including further combinations (e.g., composition of the major's antecedent with each part of the minor), we get additional results, leading to a total of five distinct derived conditionals.
The text describes how these compositions yield different subdivisions.
5
See the five results and entailments
The author states that each of the five results entails each of the other four, implying a complete logical interdependence among them.
This shows the richness of compound conditional analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالقسم الأول مثل قولنا كلَّما كان كل «جب» فلا شيء من «ط أ»، وكلما كان كل «ب أ» فكل «ده» ينتج باعتبار البساطة ثلاث نتائج؛ نتيجة التأليف بين المقدمتين، ونتيجة التأليف بين مقدم الصغرى وتالي الكبرى، ونتيجة التأليف بين تالي الصغرى ومقدم الكبرى. وباعتبار اشتماله على التأليفين أعني التأليف بين تالي الصغرى ومقدم الكبرى، والتأليف بين مقدم الصغرى وتالي الكبرى ينتج القسم الثاني من القسم الأول. وباعتبار اشتماله على التأليف بين مقدم الكبرى وكل واحد من جزأي الصغرى ينتج القسم الثاني من القسم الثاني. وباعتبار اشتماله على التأليف بين مقدم الصغرى وكل واحد من جزأي الصغرى ينتج القسم الثاني من القسم الثاني. وينتج أيضًا استلزام كل واحدة من هذه النتائج الخمس لكل واحد من الأربع الباقية.
The first subdivision is like our saying: 'Whenever every B is C, then no T is A', and 'Whenever every A is B, then every D is E'. It yields, according to simplicity, three results: the result of the composition between the two premises, the result of the composition between the minor's antecedent and the major's consequent, and the result of the composition between the minor's consequent and the major's antecedent. And according to its inclusion of the two compositions — I mean the composition between the minor's consequent and the major's antecedent, and the composition between the minor's antecedent and the major's consequent — it yields the second division of the first division. And according to its inclusion of the composition between the major's antecedent and each of the two parts of the minor, it yields the second division of the second division. And according to its inclusion of the composition between the minor's antecedent and each of the two parts of the minor, it yields the second division of the second division. And it also yields the entailment of each of these five results for each of the remaining four.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage provides an example and analysis of the first subdivision. The author gives a compound conditional syllogism with two premises: first, 'Whenever every B is C, then no T is A'; second, 'Whenever every A is B, then every D is E'. The author then explains that from a simple perspective (i.e., considering the immediate composition of the two premises), three results emerge. Then, considering more complex compositions (both combinations of parts from the two premises), additional results arise. The text describes how compositions yield divisions within divisions. Ultimately, five results are produced, and each of these results entails each of the other four, implying a complete logical interdependence.

Understanding the Third Subdivision of Conditional Syllogisms
Author gives an example of the third subdivision and its resulting conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of a compound conditional syllogism in the third subdivision and enumerate the results it yields according to simplicity and inclusion.

Core Idea
The third subdivision yields multiple layers of conclusions from a single compound premise set.

We have learned about different subdivisions of conditional syllogisms. This passage introduces the third subdivision of the fourth division. It provides a formal example using variables and lists the various results that can be derived from it. Understanding this enumeration is crucial for mastering the classification of syllogisms in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Parse the example statement
Read the Arabic: 'كلما كان لا شيء من ط أ فكل ج ب' and 'كلما كان كل ب أ وكل ج هـ'. Identify the two main premises: a universal negative conditional and a conjunctive conditional.
The second premise contains two parts: 'كل ب أ' and 'كل ج هـ'.
2
Identify the three simple results
According to 'simplicity', the author lists three results: (1) the composition of the two premises, (2) the composition of the two compositions, and (3) the composition between the minor's consequent and the major's antecedent. List these in your own words.
These are the most direct conclusions.
3
Identify the composite results based on inclusion
The author then gives results based on 'inclusion' of the sum of previous compositions. There are three more results, each corresponding to earlier divisions. Note how each is derived from combining parts of the premises.
These results show the interconnectedness of the divisions.
4
Enumerate the total implications
Finally, the author states that each of the five results entails each of the remaining four. This means there are 5×4 = 20 entailment relationships. Understand that this is a complete logical map.
This demonstrates the richness of compound syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسم الثالث هو قولنا كلما كان لا شيء من «ط أ» فكل «ج ب»، وكلَّما كان كل «ب أ» وكل «ج هـ»؛ إنه باعتبار البساطة ينتج نتيجة التأليف بين المقدمتين، ونتيجة التأليف بين التأليفين، ونتيجة التأليف بين تالي الصغرى ومقدم الكبرى. وباعتبار اشتماله على مجموع التأليفين الأولين نتيجة القسم الأول من القسم الأول. وباعتبار اشتماله على التأليف بين مقدم الكبرى وكل واحد من جزأي الصغرى نتيجة القسم الأول من القسم الثاني. وباعتبار اشتماله على التأليف بين تالي الصغرى وكل واحد من جزأي الكبرى نتيجة القسم الرابع من القسم الثاني. وينتج أيضًا استلزام كل واحد من هذه النتائج الخمس لكل واحدة من النتائج الأربع الباقية.
The third subdivision is our saying: 'Whenever nothing from T is A, then every J is B', and 'Whenever every B is A and every J is H'; according to simplicity it yields the result of the composition between the two premises, the result of the composition between the two compositions, and the result of the composition between the minor's consequent and the major's antecedent. According to its inclusion of the sum of the first two compositions, it yields the first division of the first division. According to its inclusion of the composition between the major's antecedent and each of the two parts of the minor, it yields the first division of the second division. According to its inclusion of the composition between the minor's consequent and each of the two parts of the major, it yields the fourth division of the second division. It also yields the entailment of each of these five results for each of the remaining four.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
This passage defines the third subdivision with an example: a conditional 'Whenever nothing from T is A, then every J is B' and a conjunctive premise? The second premise appears to be two conditionals combined: 'Whenever every B is A and every J is H' — but that seems like two separate statements. Perhaps it is a compound premise. The analysis again yields three simple results and then more complex ones, similar to previous subdivisions.

The Fourth Subdivision and Its Twenty-Five Results
Example and summary of the fourth subdivision results

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the example of the fourth subdivision and identify how the total of twenty-five results is derived from each subdivision.

Core Idea
Each of the four subdivisions yields exactly twenty-five results through exhaustive combination.

This passage completes the analysis of the fourth division of compound conditional syllogisms. The author provides a concrete example of the fourth subdivision and then states the final tally of results for all subdivisions. Understanding this helps you appreciate the systematic completeness of the logical classification.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the example of the fourth subdivision
The author gives: 'Whenever nothing from T is A, then every J is B' and 'Whenever every B is H, then every H is T'. Note that the minor premise (first conditional) has a negative antecedent and an affirmative consequent, while the major premise has two conditionals. Identify the terms: T, A, J, B, H.
This example is one of the complex forms in the fourth subdivision.
2
Compare its results with the third subdivision
The author says the fourth subdivision yields the same results as the third except the third result, which is replaced by a result from the second subdivision. Understand that this is due to the composition between the major's consequent and each part of the minor.
The third subdivision's results are known from earlier sections.
3
Confirm the total number of results
The author states: 'each of these four subdivisions yields twenty-five results'. This number comes from the number of possible combinations of terms and entailments. Verify by recalling previous lessons that each subdivision has a systematic enumeration.
The explanation is said to be apparent upon reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والقسم الرابع مثل قولنا كلما كان لا شيء من «ط أ» فكل «ج ب»، وكلما كان كل «ب هـ» فكل «هـ ط» ينتج باعتبار البساطة والتركيب نتائج القسم الثالث إلا النتيجة الثالثة فإنها من هذا القسم نتيجة القسم الثاني ضرورة اشتماله على التأليف بين تالي الكبرى وكل واحد من جزأي الصغرى وظهر أن كل واحد من هذه الأقسام الأربعة ينتج خمسة وعشرين نتيجة والبيان في الكل ظاهر يعرف بالتأمل.
The fourth subdivision is like our saying: 'Whenever nothing from T is A, then every J is B', and 'Whenever every B is H, then every H is T'. It yields, according to simplicity and composition, the results of the third subdivision except the third result, for it is from this subdivision the result of the second subdivision due to its necessary inclusion of the composition between the major's consequent and each of the two parts of the minor. And it has become clear that each of these four subdivisions yields twenty-five results. The explanation for all is apparent, known by reflection.
Source pages p. 229

Commentary
The fourth subdivision is given with an example: 'Whenever nothing from T is A, then every J is B' and 'Whenever every B is H, then every H is T'. The author notes that its results are the same as those of the third subdivision except that the third result is replaced by a result from the second subdivision. Finally, the author concludes that each of the four subdivisions yields twenty-five results, and this is clear upon reflection.

Key Terms
خمسة وعشرون (khamsat wa 'ishrun): Twenty-five — the total number of results from each subdivision of the fourth division of compound conditional syllogisms. [Note: Unique to this classification.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The fourth subdivision produces the same results as the third except for one result, and every subdivision yields exactly twenty-five results.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a concrete example from usul al-fiqh that fits the fourth subdivision pattern given in the passage (i.e., using 'Whenever nothing... then every...' and 'Whenever every... then every...'). Use terms like 'evidence', 'ruling', 'obligation' etc.
?

Compound Conditional Syllogisms: Second, Third, and Fourth Divisions
Author enumerates patterns and conclusions for three divisions of compound conditional syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each of the three divisions of compound conditional syllogisms and enumerate the conclusions derived from simplicity and composition.

Core Idea
Compound conditionals yield multiple conclusions through systematic composition of parts.

This passage extends the analysis of compound syllogisms formed from two conditional premises. You already know the first division; now the author presents three more patterns, each generating a set of conclusions by combining antecedents and consequents in specific ways. Understanding these patterns will deepen your ability to analyse complex conditional arguments in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the premise pattern for each division
Examine the Arabic for the second division: 'كلما كان كل ج ب فلا شيء من ط أ' and 'وكلَّما كان كل ج هـ فكل ب أ'. Note the structure: each division has two conditional premises with variables, quantifiers, and negations.
For the third and fourth divisions, locate the corresponding patterns in the text.
2
Distinguish the three conclusions by simplicity (basāṭa)
For each division, find the first three conclusions listed: (1) conjunction of the two premises, (2) conjunction of the two conjunctions, (3) a specific combination of antecedent/consequent from each premise.
In the second division, the third conclusion is 'نتيجة التأليف بين مقدم الصغرى وتالي الكبرى'.
3
Recognize the conclusions from inclusion of the first two conjunctions
Each division yields a conclusion from 'مجموع التأليفين الأولين' which is called 'نتيجة القسم الأول من القسم الأول'.
This is a reference to a typology established earlier.
4
Identify the conclusions from inclusion of parts of the minor or major
For the second division, find the conclusions from 'التأليف بين تالي الكبرى وكل واحد من جزأي الصغرى' and 'التأليف بين مقدم الصغرى وكل واحد من جزأي الكبرى'.
These correspond to specific prior subdivisions.
5
Understand the entailment relations
The text states that each of the five conclusions entails the other four, leading to a total of 25 conclusions per division (5 initial + 20 entailments). Reflect on how this expands the logical space.
The fifth conclusion is not directly enumerated but arises from the 'استلزام' relation.

The second division is like our saying: 'Whenever every J is B, then none of T is A', and 'Whenever every J is H, then every B is A'. By simplicity, it yields the conclusion of the conjunction of the two premises, the conclusion of the conjunction of the two conjunctions, and the conclusion of the conjunction of the antecedent of the minor and the consequent of the major. By its inclusion of the sum of the first two conjunctions, it yields the conclusion of the first division of the first division. By its inclusion of the conjunction of the consequent of the major with each part of the minor, it yields the conclusion of the second division of the second division. By its inclusion of the conjunction of the antecedent of the minor with each part of the major, it yields the conclusion of the third division of the second division. The entailment of each of these five conclusions yields each of the remaining four conclusions. The third division is our saying: 'Whenever none of [226r] T is A, then every J is B', and 'Whenever every B is A and every J is H'. By simplicity, it yields the conclusion of the conjunction of the two premises, the conclusion of the conjunction of the two conjunctions, and the conclusion of the conjunction of the consequent of the minor and the antecedent of the major. By its inclusion of the sum of the first two conjunctions, it yields the conclusion of the first division of the first division. By its inclusion of the conjunction of the antecedent of the major with each part of the minor, it yields the conclusion of the first division of the second division. By its inclusion of the conjunction of the consequent of the minor with each part of the major, it yields the conclusion of the fourth division of the second division. It also yields the entailment of each of these five conclusions for each of the remaining four conclusions. The fourth division is like our saying: 'Whenever none of T is A, then every J is B', and 'Whenever every B is H, then every H is T'. It yields, by simplicity and composition, the conclusions of the third division except the third conclusion, for it belongs to this division as the conclusion of the second division due to its necessary inclusion of the conjunction of the consequent of the major with each part of the minor. It is clear that each of these four divisions yields twenty-five conclusions, and the explanation in all is obvious, known by reflection.

Commentary
This passage describes the second, third, and fourth divisions of compound syllogisms constructed from two conditional premises (mutasilat). Each division presents a specific pattern of premises and then enumerates the conclusions derivable from that pattern. The author treats the 'simplicity' (al-basāṭa) as the direct composition of the premises, and then more complex compositions involving conjunctions of parts of the premises. The number of conclusions is systematically counted: for each division, there are five initial conclusions from different aspects (simple conjunction, conjunction of conjunctions, conjunction of antecedent/consequent of one with parts of the other, etc.), and then the entailment relationships among these five yield additional ones, totaling five initial plus the entailments, but the final count is twenty-five per division. The examples use variable letters (ج, ب, أ, etc.) as placeholders for terms, typical in logical notation. The author assumes the reader is familiar with the first division mentioned elsewhere, as he references 'the first division of the first division' etc. In an Islamic context, one could analogize: if a conditional premise is like 'If it is intoxicating (J), then it is forbidden (B)' and another like 'If it is forbidden (B), then it is impure (A)', the composition would yield conclusions about the relationship between intoxication and impurity, but here the premises are more complex with negative and universal quantifiers.

Critique of Compound Syllogisms from Conditionals and Disjunctives
Author critiques claimed productivity rules for compound syllogisms from disjunctives and conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a compound syllogism from two conditionals or disjunctives is genuinely productive by identifying its reliance on contingent actual truths.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms from conditionals or disjunctives only appear productive; true certainty requires necessary connections, not contingent truth.

Earlier logicians claimed that compound syllogisms formed from two disjunctive or two conditional premises could yield categorical conclusions under certain sharing conditions. In this passage, the author scrutinizes these claims, arguing that such syllogisms are not inherently productive. The central issue is that the truth of a conditional does not require the actual truth of its parts, and the supposed conclusions often depend on contingent facts that may not hold. This lesson will walk through the author's critique and a concrete counterexample, showing why these compound forms fail to provide the certainty required in demonstrative reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claimed rules
The author first describes the earlier logicians' position: when two disjunctives share a complete part, six conclusions supposedly follow; and there are reductions to conditional forms. Identify the 'complete part' as a full term appearing identically in both premises.
These rules are not the author's own view; they are the target of critique.
2
Grasp the core objection
The author argues that certainty about the conclusion requires a necessary connection that is not evident in these compound forms. Moreover, the truth of a conditional does not imply the truth of its parts, so a conclusion that depends on the actual truth of antecedents may fail.
The author emphasizes that what is productive in itself must guarantee the conclusion without external conditions.
3
Examine the counterexample
Consider: 'Whenever every human is a writer, then every rational being is sighted; whenever every rational being is sighted, then every writer is sighted.' The claimed conclusion 'Every human is sighted' does not follow, because there exist humans who are not sighted. The premises may be true even when the conclusion is false.
This shows the syllogism is not universally productive; it only works if the conclusion happens to be true, but not by logical necessity.
4
Analyze the incomplete sharing case
When the sharing is in an incomplete part (e.g., 'potentially laughing'), the syllogism requires additional categorical premises to be actually true (e.g., 'every human is laughing'). Since these actualities hold only sometimes, the syllogism is not productive by itself but only by means of external facts.
The author concludes that such syllogisms are not productive per se, but only conditionally productive in some forms.

Nor does it follow from the truth of the conclusion the truth of what is productive, for it is among the necessary consequences; and it does not follow from the truth of the necessary consequence the truth of what necessitates it in actual fact, especially when the conclusion is categorical in conditional syllogisms as previously. And there may be conditionals that are true while none of their parts are true in actual fact at all. If it were like that, then accidents would have a role in every division mentioned in this chapter. Thus it is said in the syllogism composed of two conditionals that if it were productive of a categorical conclusion it would be productive in all forms; but it is not so, for if we say 'Whenever every human is a writer, then every rational being is sighted, and whenever every rational being is sighted, then every writer is sighted' then it does not yield our statement 'Every human is sighted'; and there have been humans who are not sighted. This is when the sharing is in a complete part. Likewise when the sharing is in an incomplete part, for although it may appear in some forms to be productive, it is not productive by itself but by means of what is composed of the two categoricals, as in our statement 'Whenever every human is potentially laughing, then every laughing being is rational, and whenever every rational being is intelligent, then every intelligent being is sensitive' then it does not yield 'Every human is sensitive' unless it is actually the case that every human is laughing, and every laughing being is rational. And it is also actually the case that every rational being is intelligent, and every intelligent being is sensitive, as previously. And the actuality of each of them is only in some forms. Therefore it is not productive by means of another except in some forms. And there is no doubt that if it is productive by means of another, then it is not productive by itself. Since the corruption appeared in the conditionals, it also appeared in the disjunctives, for the syllogism composed of two disjunctives is only productive by means of what is composed of two conditionals, as mentioned in the examples that were given. Likewise in the two different [types], as in our statement 'Whenever a thing is at rest, it is a substance, and it is absolutely not either a substance or moving' then it does not ...
Source pages p. 230

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of compound syllogisms formed from two disjunctive or two conditional premises, arguing that the productivity rules claimed by earlier logicians are not universally sound. The author first describes the supposed rules: when two disjunctives share a complete part (i.e., a full term), six conclusions are supposed to follow, and various reductions to conditional forms are proposed. He then notes that these ideas come from earlier thinkers but are problematic. The core critique is that certainty of the conclusion requires a necessary connection, which is not obvious in these complex forms. Moreover, truth of a conditional does not require truth of its parts, so syllogisms that rely on actual truth of antecedents fail when those antecedents are not actually true. The author provides counterexamples: from the premise 'Whenever every human is a writer, then every rational is sighted' and 'Whenever every rational is sighted, then every writer is sighted' it does not follow that 'Every human is sighted' because not all humans are sighted. Similarly, with incomplete sharing, the syllogism only works if additional categorical statements are actually true, which is not guaranteed. The same critique applies to disjunctives because they depend on conditionals. The passage ends with an example involving 'rest', 'substance', and 'moving'. Overall, the author demonstrates that these compound syllogisms are not productive in themselves but only conditionally, and thus the earlier claimed rules are flawed.

The Syllogism
intermediate
10 min
Compound Conditional Syllogisms Are Not Independently Productive
Author demonstrates why compound conditional syllogisms fail to produce a conclusion without mediation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why compound conditional syllogisms fail to directly produce a categorical conclusion and explain the role of mediation by categorical premises.

Core Idea
Compound conditionals only appear productive when hidden categorical premises are at work.

In this passage, the author examines compound conditional syllogisms—those made of two conditional premises—and shows why they do not independently yield a categorical conclusion. Through a series of examples, he demonstrates that any seeming productivity actually relies on separate categorical premises. This lesson will walk you through his reasoning and equip you to recognise when a conditional argument secretly depends on unstated assumptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main claim
The author argues that compound conditional syllogisms are not independently productive; they require mediation by categorical premises. Look for the phrase 'لا يكون منتجا بنفسه' (not productive by itself).
This sets the thesis of the passage.
2
Analyze the first counterexample (complete sharing)
Consider the connected conditionals: 'Whenever every human is a writer, then every speaker is sighted; and whenever every speaker is sighted, then every writer is sighted.' The author shows that the conclusion 'Every human is sighted' does not follow, because some humans are not writers. Note the logical structure: both premises share a complete part (the terms writer and sighted).
The sharing is 'tām' (complete) in the middle term.
3
Examine the second scenario (incomplete sharing)
Now consider incomplete sharing: 'Whenever every human is potentially laughing, then every laugher is a speaker; and whenever every speaker is rational, then every rational being is sentient.' This does not yield 'Every human is sentient' unless additional categorical propositions like 'Every human is laughing' are verified. Recognize that this verification is only possible in some cases, so the syllogism is not independently productive.
Incomplete sharing is 'ghayr tām'.
4
Extend to disjoined and mixed conditionals
The author states that the same principle applies to disjoined conditionals (munfaṣila) and mixed types. For example, 'Whenever a thing is stationary, it is a substance; and absolutely, it is either a substance or in motion' does not produce a correct categorical conclusion without mediation. Understand why: the disjunction itself does not provide the needed categorical link.
Disjoined conditionals require conversion to connected conditionals for productivity.
5
Synthesize the overall lesson
Conclude that compound conditional syllogisms are never independently productive. Any apparent output depends on external categorical premises that verify the antecedents. This is a fundamental principle for evaluating arguments in Islamic logic.
This principle guards against invalid inferences in theological and legal reasoning.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النتيجة أيضًا صدق ما يكون منتجا؛ إذ هو من اللوازم، ولا يلزم من صدق اللازم صدق ما يكون مستلزما له في نفس الأمر على الخصوص إذا كانت النتيجة حملية في الأقيسة الشرطية كما مر . وقد يكون من الشرطيات ما يكون صادقًا من غير أن يصدق جزء من أجزائه في نفس الأمر أصلا. ولو كان كذلك لكان للأعراض مدخل في كل قسم من الأقسام المذكورة في هذا الفصل فيقال في المؤلف من المتصلتين إنه إذ كان منتجا نتيجة حملية كان منتجا في جميع الصور؛ وليس كذلك فإنا إذا قلنا كلَّما كان كل إنسان كاتبا كان كل ناطق مدركًا بالبصر، وكلَّما كان كل ناطق مدركًا بالبصر كان كل كاتب مدركًا بالبصر فإنه لا ينتج قولنا كل إنسان مدرك بالبصر؛ وقد كان من الإنسان ما لا يكون مدركًا بالبصر : هذا إذا كان الاشتراك في جزء تام. وكذلك إذا كان الاشتراك في جزء غير تام فإنه وإن كان موهما في البعض من الصور أن يكون منتجا فذلك لا يكون منتجا بنفسه بل بواسطة ما يكون مؤلفًا من الحمليتين كما في قولنا كلَّما كان كل إنسان ضاحكًا بالقوة كان كل ضاحك ناطقا، وكلَّما كان كل ناطق عاقلا كان كل عاقل حساسا فإنه لا ينتج قولنا كل إنسان حساس إلا وأن يتحقق في نفس الأمر أن كل إنسان ضاحك، وكل ضاحك ناطق. ويتحقق أيضًا أن كل ناطق عاقل، وكل عاقل حساس كما مر . وتحقق كل واحد منهما لا يكون إلا في البعض من الصور؛ فإذن لا يكون منتجا بواسطة الغير إلا في البعض من الصور. ولا يستراب في أنه إذا كان منتجا بواسطة الغير فلا يكون منتجا بنفسه. ولما ظهر الفساد في المتصلتين فقد ظهر في المنفصلتين أيضًا؛ إذ المؤلف من المنفصلتين لا يكون منتجاً إلا بواسطة ما يكون مؤلفًا من المتصلتين كما سبق ذكره في الأمثلة التي مرَّ ذكرها. وكذلك في المختلفتين كما في قولنا كلَّما كان الشيء ساكنا كان جوهرا، وليس البتة إما أن يكون جوهرًا أو متحركًا فإنه لا ينتج سلب الانفصال المانع من الخلو بين الطرفين مع التباين بينهما وهو كونه ساكنا متحرّكًا. والمعنى من السكون هنا عدم الحركة عما من شأنه أن يتحرك. ولا يقال هب أنه كذلك لكن لم قلتم بأنه إذا كان ساكنا كان جوهرا. فإنه إذا لم يكن جوهرًا كان عرضًا؛ فنقول في العرض كما قلناه في الجوهر. وعلى هذا في الغير من الأقيسة المذكورة في هذا الباب؛ والله أعلم بالصواب.
The result is also true of what is productive, for it is among the necessary consequences. But from the truth of the necessary consequence, the truth of what is necessitated by it does not necessarily follow, in reality, especially when the result is categorical in conditional syllogisms, as has passed. And there may be among conditionals what is true without any part of its parts being true in reality at all. If it were otherwise, then accidents would have an entry in every division mentioned in this chapter. It is said in the compound of two connected conditionals that if it were productive of a categorical result, it would be productive in all forms. But it is not so, for if we say: 'Whenever every human is a writer, then every speaker is sighted; and whenever every speaker is sighted, then every writer is sighted,' then it does not produce our statement 'Every human is sighted,' and some humans are not sighted. This is when the sharing is in a complete part. And likewise if the sharing is in an incomplete part, then even if it might seem in some forms that it is productive, that is not productive by itself but by means of what is composed of the two categoricals, as in our statement: 'Whenever every human is potentially laughing, then every laugher is a speaker; and whenever every speaker is rational, then every rational being is sentient,' then it does not produce our statement 'Every human is sentient' unless it is verified in reality that every human is laughing and every laugher is a speaker, and it is also verified that every speaker is rational and every rational being is sentient, as has passed. The verification of each one of them occurs only in some forms. Therefore, it is productive by means of another only in some forms, and there is no doubt that if it is productive by means of another, then it is not productive by itself. And when the corruption appeared in the connected conditionals, it also appeared in the disjoined conditionals, for the compound of two disjoined conditionals is not productive except by means of what is composed of the two connected conditionals, as previously mentioned in the examples that have passed. And likewise in the mixed types, as in our statement: 'Whenever a thing is stationary, it is a substance; and absolutely, it is either a substance or in motion,' then it does not produce the negation of the disjunction that prevents emptiness between the two extremes with the distinction between them, which is its being stationary and moving. The meaning of stationariness here is the absence of motion from that which is apt to move. And it is not said: 'Grant that it is so, but why do you say that if it is stationary then it is a substance?' For if it is not a substance, then it is an accident; so we say about the accident as we said about the substance. And upon this pattern are the rest of the syllogisms mentioned in this chapter. And Allah knows best what is correct.
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage demonstrates why compound conditional syllogisms (composed of two conditional premises) do not directly yield a categorical conclusion. The author begins by stating a logical principle: the truth of a necessary consequence does not guarantee the truth of its antecedent, especially when the conclusion is categorical. In conditional syllogisms, the premises may be true even if no part corresponds to reality. The author then provides a concrete example using two connected conditionals (متصلة): 'Whenever every human is a writer, then every speaker is sighted' and 'Whenever every speaker is sighted, then every writer is sighted.' The supposed conclusion 'Every human is sighted' does not follow because some humans are not sighted. This is the case when the sharing between the two premises is in a complete part (اشتراك في جزء تام). Next, the author considers incomplete sharing (جزء غير تام) with another example: 'Whenever every human is potentially laughing, then every laugher is a speaker; and whenever every speaker is rational, then every rational being is sentient.' This does not yield 'Every human is sentient' unless additional categorical propositions are verified (e.g., 'Every human is laughing'). Since such verification is only possible in some forms, the syllogism is not independently productive. The author then extends the argument to disjoined conditionals (منفصلات) and mixed cases, showing that without mediation by categorical premises, no direct product is obtained. The passage concludes that these compound conditionals are not productive by themselves, and any appearance of productivity is due to external categorical premises.

The Syllogism
